Allah as a Proper Name for the Necessary Existent
The commentator defines 'Allah' as a proper name for the necessary existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'Allah' as a personal proper name for the necessarily existent essence, and the distinction between the name and its descriptive specification.

Core Idea
Allah is a unique personal name, not a description.

The commentator begins by defining the most fundamental word in Islamic theology: Allah. He clarifies that this name is not derived from any attribute but is a proper name pointing directly to the necessary existent. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between the name itself and the descriptions we use to refer to the same reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once. Notice the key terms: علم, الذات, الواجب الوجود, علم شخصي.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the definition
Identify the three components of the definition: proper name, essence (dhat), necessary existent (wajib al-wujud), deserving all praise.
3
Grasp the key distinction
Focus on the commentator's clarification: 'wajib al-wujud' specifies the named, not part of the named. This prevents 'Allah' from being a universal concept.
This is the main point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله : علم على الذات الواجب الوجود المستحق لجميع المحامد. وقولنا : (الواجب الوجود ...) إلى آخره : تعيين للمسمى، لا أنه من جملة المسمَّى على ما هو التحقيق وإلا لكان كليًّا، وهو علم شخصي؛ بمعنى أن مدلوله معيَّن في الخارج، لا بمعنى أنه قامت به مشخصات كالطول والبياض وهكذا.
And Allah: a proper name for the essence that necessarily exists, deserving all praise. And our statement: 'the necessarily existent... etc.' is a specification of the named, not that it is part of the named according to the verified position; otherwise it would be a universal. And it is a personal proper name, meaning its referent is specified in external existence, not in the sense that it is qualified by individuators like height and whiteness and so on.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'Allah'. The commentator states that it is a proper name (alam) for the essence that is necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud) and deserving of all praise. He clarifies that when we describe God as 'the necessarily existent', this is only a specification of the named entity, not that the description is part of the named entity itself; otherwise the name would become a universal concept. He emphasizes that 'Allah' is a personal proper name (alam shakhsi), meaning it refers to a specific entity in external reality, not merely a concept. However, he notes that this personal designation is not like human personal names that involve physical characteristics; rather, it refers to the unique divine essence.

Key Terms
الله
الذات
الواجب الوجود
المحامد
علم شخصي
كلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'wajib al-wujud' is part of the definition of 'Allah'.
Correction: The commentator clarifies it is only a specification, not part of the name itself.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator mean when he says 'Allah' is a personal proper name (alam shakhsi)?
A) It refers to a concept in the mind.
B) It refers to a specific entity in external existence, but not in the way humans have physical individuators.
C) It is derived from attributes.
D) It is a universal term.

Key Takeaway
Allah is a unique personal proper name for the necessary existent essence, and descriptions like 'wajib al-wujud' are specifications, not parts of the name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you utter the name 'Allah', do you truly sense that you are addressing the unique Necessary Existent, or has it become a routine word? Commit to pausing before each mention of His name today, reminding yourself that this name points to the One who alone deserves all praise.
?
If Allah's name is not derived from attributes, what does that imply about your tendency to conceptualize Him using human categories? In what specific area of your life have you been limiting your understanding of Allah to your own ideas? Repent and resolve to learn about Him as He has revealed Himself.
?
The commentator distinguishes between a personal proper name and a universal concept. Have you ever reduced Allah to a mere concept in your mind, something you discuss rather than someone you encounter? How will you transform your knowledge of 'Allah' into a living relationship starting today?

Al-Rahman and Al-Rahim: Attributes of Beneficence
Al-Laqqani explains the two names and their distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Rahman and al-Rahim are interpreted as attributes of beneficence rather than emotional mercy, and how they differ in scope.

Core Idea
God’s mercy is beneficence, not emotion.

Before we recite the basmala, we must grasp the profound meaning of the two divine names it contains. The author, al-Laqqani, clarifies that al-Rahman and al-Rahim are not about emotional tenderness – an impossibility for Allah – but about His beneficence. This passage unpacks their distinct connotations and the wisdom behind pairing them together.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Note the author's initial correction of the meaning of 'rahma'.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا بمعناها الأصلي'.
2
Understand the correction of 'rahma'
The author first redefines 'rahma' in reference to Allah as beneficence or the will to do good, not emotional tenderness. This is a crucial point of tanzih (transcendence).
3
Distinguish between the two names
Identify how al-Rahman and al-Rahim differ: one relates to great favors, the other to subtle favors. The author cites the principle that increase in form indicates increase in meaning.
The forms 'فَعْلان' and 'فَعِيل' both indicate intensity.
4
Grasp the wisdom of pairing them
The combination in the basmala teaches that we should ask Allah for both great and small favors, as all are from Him alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و(الرَّحمنِ الرَّحيم) صفتان مأخوذتان من الرحمة بمعنى الإحسان، أو إرادة الإحسان، لا بمعناها الأصلي الذي هو رقّةٌ في القلب تقتضي التفضل والإحسان؛ لاستحالة ذلك في حقِّهِ تعالى، فالرحمن الرحيمُ في حقِّهِ تعالى بمعنى المحسن أو مريد الإحسان، لكن الأول بمعنى المحسن بجلائل النعم؛ أي: بالنعم الجليلة، والثاني بمعنى المحسن بدقائق النعم؛ أي: بالنعم الدقيقة؛ لأنَّ زيادة المبنى على زيادة المعنى غالباً، وإنما جمع بينهما إشارةً إلى أنه ينبغي أن يُطلب منه تعالى النعمُ الحقيرة كما ينبغي أن يُطلب منه النعم العظيمة؛ لأن الكلَّ منه وحده سبحانه وتعالى. ويتعلَّقُ بالبسملة أبحاث كثيرة، فلا نطيلُ بذكرها.
And 'al-Rahman al-Rahim' are two attributes derived from mercy (al-rahma) meaning beneficence (al-ihsan) or the will to do good, not in its original meaning which is tenderness in the heart necessitating favor and beneficence; because that is impossible in His right, exalted is He. So al-Rahman and al-Rahim in His right, exalted is He, mean the benefactor or the one who intends beneficence, but the first means the benefactor with great favors, i.e. with magnificent favors, and the second means the benefactor with subtle favors, i.e. with delicate favors, because increase in form often indicates increase in meaning. And they are combined to indicate that one should ask from Him both insignificant favors as well as great favors, because all are from Him alone, glorified and exalted. And there are many investigations related to the basmala, but we will not prolong by mentioning them.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage explains the two names 'al-Rahman' and 'al-Rahim' as attributes derived from 'rahma' (mercy). The author clarifies that 'rahma' in this context does not mean emotional tenderness (which implies need and is impossible for Allah), but rather 'beneficence' (الإحسان) or 'the will to do good'. Both names therefore mean 'the Benefactor' (المحسن) or 'the One who intends beneficence'. However, there is a distinction: 'al-Rahman' refers to beneficence with great and magnificent favors (جلائل النعم), while 'al-Rahim' refers to beneficence with subtle and delicate favors (دقائق النعم). This distinction is based on the principle that increase in form often indicates increase in meaning (زيادة المبنى على زيادة المعنى), since the form 'فَعِل' is more intensive than 'فاعل'. The combination of both names in the basmala is a sign that one should ask Allah for both small and great favors, because all favors come from Him alone. The author concludes by noting that there are many other investigations related to the basmala but chooses not to elaborate.

Key Terms
الرحمن
الرحيم
الرحمة
الإحسان
جلائل النعم
دقائق النعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Allah has emotional mercy like human tenderness.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'rahma' for Allah means beneficence, not emotional tenderness, because the latter implies need and change, which are impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does 'al-Rahman' indicate great favors while 'al-Rahim' indicates subtle favors?
A) Because the names are synonyms.
B) Because increase in form often indicates increase in meaning.
C) Because Allah prefers great favors over subtle ones.
D) Because the basmala requires two different names.

Key Takeaway
Al-Rahman and al-Rahim both signify Allah's beneficence, but al-Rahman pertains to great favors and al-Rahim to subtle favors, reminding us to rely on Allah for all needs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you only bring your 'big' problems to Allah in prayer, neglecting the small daily concerns? Will you today begin to ask Him for both the magnificent and the minute, trusting that He cares about every detail of your life?
?
When hardship strikes and you struggle to feel Allah's mercy, does this teaching—that His mercy is beneficence, not emotion—help you trust that He is acting for your good even when you cannot feel it? How will you remind yourself of this truth this week?
?
Are you guilty of imagining Allah's mercy as a soft, human-like feeling that can fluctuate? Based on this lesson, what must you change in your understanding to worship Him as He truly is, free from anthropomorphism?

Meanings of al-Rahman and al-Rahim
Definition and distinction of al-Rahman and al-Rahim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meanings of al-Rahman and al-Rahim as divine attributes of beneficence, their distinction, and why they are combined in the basmalah.

Core Idea
Al-Rahman and al-Rahim signify beneficence, not human emotion.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies two of the most frequently recited names of Allah: al-Rahman and al-Rahim. Avoiding a common misunderstanding that they imply compassion like ours, he explains that they derive from the root mercy but refer to the act of bestowing blessings. This lesson unpacks the precise meanings and the wisdom behind their pairing in the basmalah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation, to get a sense of the author's argument.
Notice the key terms al-Rahman, al-Rahim, and al-ihsan.
2
Understand the ta'wil of mercy
The author explains that mercy here means beneficence, not emotional softness. This is because emotional softness is impossible for Allah.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
3
Distinguish between the two names
Al-Rahman indicates bestowal of great blessings, al-Rahim of subtle blessings. The principle of 'increase in form indicates increase in meaning' supports this.
The distinction is based on the intensity of the derived forms.
4
Learn the reason for their combination
They are combined to teach that one should ask Allah for both great and small needs, for all come from Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و(الرَّحمنِ الرَّحيم) صفتان مأخوذتان من الرحمة بمعنى الإحسان، أو إرادة الإحسان، لا بمعناها الأصلي الذي هو رقَّةٌ في القلب تقتضي التفضل والإحسان؛ لاستحالة ذلك في حقِّهِ تعالى، فالرحمن الرحيمُ في حقِّهِ تعالى بمعنى المحسن أو مريد الإحسان، لكن الأول بمعنى المحسن بجلائل النعم؛ أي: بالنعم الجليلة، والثاني بمعنى المحسن بدقائق النعم؛ أي: بالنعم الدقيقة؛ لأنَّ زيادة المبنى على زيادة المعنى غالباً (۳) ، وإنما جمع بينهما إشارةً إلى أنه ينبغي أن يُطلب منه تعالى النعمُ الحقيرة كما ينبغي أن يُطلب منه النعم العظيمة (4) ؛ لأن الكلَّ منه وحده سبحانه وتعالى. ويتعلَّقُ بالبسملة أبحاث كثيرة، فلا نطيلُ بذكرها .
And (al-Rahman, al-Rahim) are two attributes derived from mercy in the sense of beneficence, or the will to do good, not in its original meaning which is softness in the heart necessitating favor and beneficence; because that is impossible in His right, the Exalted. So al-Rahman and al-Rahim in the right of the Exalted are in the sense of the Benefactor or the Willer of good, but the first is in the sense of the Benefactor with great blessings – i.e., with glorious blessings – and the second is in the sense of the Benefactor with subtle blessings – i.e., with fine blessings – because increase in form indicates increase in meaning generally (3). They are only combined to indicate that one should seek from Him, the Exalted, insignificant blessings as one should seek from Him great blessings (4); because all is from Him alone, glorified and exalted. And many investigations relate to the basmalah, but we will not lengthen by mentioning them.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines the two divine names al-Rahman and al-Rahim, explaining that they are derived from the root 'rahmah' but not in the human sense of emotional tenderness. Instead, they signify beneficence (ihsan) or the will to do good (iradat al-ihsan), as emotional softness is impossible for Allah. The author then distinguishes between the two: al-Rahman indicates the bestower of great and obvious blessings, while al-Rahim indicates the bestower of subtle and fine blessings. This distinction is supported by the linguistic principle that longer forms often carry greater intensity. The two names are combined to teach that one should ask Allah for both great and small needs, for all come from Him alone. The passage concludes by noting that the basmalah contains many further investigations which are omitted for brevity.

Key Terms
الرحمن
الرحيم
الرحمة
الإحسان
المحسن
مريد الإحسان
جليل النعم
دقيق النعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Rahman and al-Rahim mean that Allah has emotional mercy like human compassion.
Correction: The passage clarifies that mercy here means beneficence (ihsan) or the will to do good, because emotional softness is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'al-Rahman' mean when applied to Allah?
A) The One who has tender mercy
B) The One who bestows great blessings or intends good
C) The One who is praised
D) The One who is eternal

Key Takeaway
Al-Rahman and al-Rahim are attributes of beneficence, with al-Rahman referring to bestowing great blessings and al-Rahim to subtle blessings, and their combination teaches us to ask Allah for all needs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Rahman and al-Rahim, do you truly believe that He is the source of both your greatest blessings and your smallest needs? Or do you unconsciously attribute the small things to your own efforts?
?
This lesson teaches that Allah's mercy is not an emotion but active beneficence. Does your gratitude reflect that you see His hand in both the big and small gifts? Or do you only thank Him for the obvious ones?
?
The combination of the two names invites you to ask Allah for everything. What is one small need you have hesitated to bring before Him because you thought it too trivial? Will you ask Him for it now?

Distinction Between Dhat and Shakhs
The author argues against applying 'shakhs' to Allah and distinguishes it from 'dhat'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'shakhs' cannot be used for Allah and how 'dhat' is the correct theological term for His essence.

Core Idea
Allah is not a 'person' in the physical sense; 'dhat' is the precise term for His essence.

This lesson clarifies a subtle but crucial linguistic distinction in Islamic theology. The author explains why 'shakhs' (person) should not be applied to Allah, citing linguists and a hadith, and establishes that 'dhat' (essence) is the proper term. Understanding this helps avoid anthropomorphic misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the argument flow: the author first states the rule, then provides evidence, then a definition.
Pay attention to the key terms: ذات, شخص, مشخصات.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
The author argues that 'shakhs' refers to a physical body with height (جسم مؤلف له شخوص وارتفاع). Therefore it cannot apply to Allah or His attributes. Note the citation from al-Khattabi.
The hadith 'لا شخص أغير من الله' uses 'شخص' metaphorically, not literally.
3
Grasp the general-specific relationship
Al-Jurjani's definition shows that 'dhat' is more general than 'shakhs' – dhat covers both corporeal and incorporeal, while shakhs is only for bodies. This is the key takeaway.
Thus when speaking of Allah, we say 'ذات' (essence) not 'شخص'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاشتقاق منه ، فلا يقال لصفاته تعالى : مشخصات، فقد ذكر الخطابي وغيره أنه لا يسمى شخصاً إلا جسم مؤلف له شخوص وارتفاع كما في التاج » ( ش خ (ص) ، وقال: (وفي الحديث : «لا شخص أغير من الله» [رواه مسلم (١٤۹۹]، قال ابن الأثير : الشخص : كل جسم له ارتفاع وظهور، والمراد يه إثبات الذات فاستعير لها لفظ الشخص) ، فنقول ذات ،وصفات للتباين اللغوي والاصطلاحي بين الذات والشخص، قال العلامة الجرجاني في تعريفاته) (ص ۱۰۷): (الذات أعم من الشخص ؛ لأن الذات تُطلق على الجسم وغيره والشخص لا يُطلق إلا على الجسم).
From its derivation, so one does not say for His attributes 'persons'. Al-Khattabi and others mentioned that nothing is called a person except a composed body having stature and height, as in al-Taj (sh.kh.s). And he said: 'In the hadith: 'There is no one more jealous than Allah' [Muslim 1499]. Ibn al-Athir said: 'Al-shakhs: every body having height and appearance, and the intent is to affirm the essence, so the word 'shakhs' was borrowed for it.' So we say 'dhat' (essence) and 'sifat' (attributes) due to the linguistic and terminological difference between dhat and shakhs. Al-Allamah al-Jurjani said in his Definitions (p. 107): 'Dhat is more general than shakhs, because dhat is applied to body and other than body, while shakhs is only applied to body.'
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between the terms 'dhat' (essence) and 'shakhs' (person) to prevent misunderstanding. The author argues that the word 'shakhs' should not be used for Allah or His attributes because linguistically it refers to a physical body with height and form. He cites al-Khattabi and others to support this, noting that even the hadith 'la shakhs aghyar min Allah' uses 'shakhs' metaphorically to affirm the essence, not literally. The author then quotes al-Jurjani's definition: 'dhat' is more general than 'shakhs' because it applies to both corporeal and incorporeal beings, whereas 'shakhs' applies only to bodies. Thus, when speaking of Allah, one should use 'dhat' and 'sifat' rather than 'shakhs'.

Key Terms
ذات
شخص
مشخصات
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah can be described as a 'person' in a physical sense.
Correction: This passage guards against the misconception that Allah is a 'person' with a physical body. The term 'shakhs' implies a composed body with stature, which cannot apply to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, how are dhat and shakhs related?
A) Dhat is a type of shakhs
B) Dhat is more general than shakhs
C) They are synonyms
D) Shakhs is more general than dhat

Key Takeaway
The term 'shakhs' implies a physical body, so it cannot be used for Allah; 'dhat' is the correct, general term for His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unknowingly imagined Allah as having a form or shape, even in your mind? After learning that 'shakhs' implies a body, will you consciously replace such thoughts with the term 'dhat' to honor His transcendence?
?
The hadith says 'No one is more jealous than Allah.' Has your own jealousy ever caused you to project human emotions onto Allah? How can you ensure your understanding of His attributes remains pure of anthropomorphism?
?
Ibn al-Athir explained that 'shakhs' in the hadith is metaphorical. Are there other descriptions of Allah you might be taking too literally? Commit today to studying the proper methodology of interpreting Allah's attributes.

The Broad Meaning of 'Voluntary' in Hamd
Objection and answer: how 'voluntary' includes essence and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'voluntary' (ikhtiyari) is interpreted broadly to include praise of Allah's essence and attributes.

Core Idea
Voluntary can mean real, source, or inseparable—not just an act of choice.

The definition of hamd restricts it to voluntary good, but what about praising Allah's essence and attributes, which are not voluntary? This objection is addressed by interpreting 'voluntary' in a broader sense. In this lesson, we explore that interpretation and its three categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage (the objection) carefully. Understand why someone might think praising Allah's essence and attributes is not hamd.
The objection is based on a literal understanding of 'voluntary'.
2
Identify the three categories in the answer
The answer classifies 'voluntary' into three types: real (haqiqa), by ruling (hukman) as source, and by ruling as inseparable. Find each in the text.
3
Apply the categories to Allah's attributes
Consider examples: essence as source, attributes of effect as source, attributes of non-effect as inseparable. How does this resolve the objection?
Connects to learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : التقييد بالاختياري يخرج الحمد على ذاته تعالى وصفاته، فظاهره أنه لا يسمى حمداً، والتزمه بعضهم وقال يسمى مدحاً. قلت: أجيب عن ذلك بأن المراد ما يشمل الاختياري حقيقة وهو ظاهر، أو حكماً والمراد به ما كان منشأ للأفعال الاختيارية؛ كالذات وصفات التأثير، أو ملازماً للمنشا؛ كصفات غير التأثير.
If you say: The restriction by 'voluntary' excludes praise of His essence, exalted, and His attributes; so apparently it is not called hamd, and some have committed to that and said it is called madh. I say: The answer to that is that what is meant includes what is voluntary in reality (haqiqa), which is apparent, or by ruling (hukman), by which is meant what is a source (mansha') for voluntary acts, like the essence and attributes of effect (sifat al-ta'thir), or what is inseparable from the source, like attributes that are not of effect (sifat ghayr al-ta'thir).
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection: since the definition of hamd restricts it to voluntary good, praising Allah's essence and His eternal attributes would not be hamd, because they are not voluntary. Some scholars accepted this implication and said such praise is called madh. The author's answer: the term 'voluntary' (ikhtiyari) is interpreted broadly. It includes: (1) that which is voluntary in reality (haqiqa) — which is apparent, but not applicable to the essence; (2) that which is voluntary by ruling (hukman) — meaning what is the source (mansha') of voluntary actions, such as the essence and the attributes of effect (like will and power); (3) that which is inseparable from that source (mulazim li al-mansha'), such as attributes of non-effect (like life and knowledge). Thus, praise of Allah's essence and attributes is included under this broad interpretation.

Key Terms
اختياري (ikhtiyari)
حقيقة (haqiqa)
حكم (hukm)
منشأ (mansha')
صفات التأثير (sifat al-ta'thir)
صفات غير التأثير (sifat ghayr al-ta'thir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think praising Allah's essence and attributes is not truly hamd because they are not voluntary.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'voluntary' is interpreted broadly to include the source of voluntary actions and what is inseparable from it, so such praise is indeed hamd.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, which category includes Allah's essence and attributes of effect?
A) Voluntary in reality (haqiqa)
B) Voluntary by ruling as source (mansha')
C) Voluntary by ruling as inseparable (mulazim)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The restriction 'voluntary' in hamd is interpreted broadly to include praise of Allah's essence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah in your daily life, do you only thank Him for what you consider your own voluntary choices? How does this lesson challenge you to expand your praise to include His essence and attributes? Write down one attribute of Allah you will intentionally praise today in your prayer.
?
Imagine you have been limiting your hamd to only the good deeds you choose to do. What does that reveal about your understanding of Allah's mercy and generosity? Will you now include thanks for His very existence and perfect attributes? Commit to reciting Al-Fatiha with renewed awareness that every verse contains hamd of His essence and attributes.

The Legal Definition of Tawhid and Its Three Aspects
legal definition of tawhid with three aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the legal (sharʿī) definition of tawhid and its three categories: oneness of essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
Tawhid is not just worship; it is affirming Allah's oneness in essence, attributes, and actions.

This passage from al-Sanūsī's poem establishes what tawhid means in the legal sense, moving beyond mere ritual worship to include core beliefs about Allah's essence, attributes, and actions. It also introduces an alternative definition that guards against both anthropomorphism and the Mu'tazilite negation of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for familiarity, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a definition followed by elaboration on three categories.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three aspects of oneness
Find where the author discusses oneness of essence, attributes, and actions. Note what each entails (e.g., essence does not divide, attributes are not multiple of same kind, actions are created only by Allah).
3
Understand the alternative definition
The passage ends with another definition: affirmation of an essence that neither resembles nor is stripped of attributes. Reflect on how this corrects both the anthropomorphists and the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد به هنا الشرعي (1) ، لا بمعنى الفن المدوّن فيما سيأتي، وهو (2) : إفراد المعبودِ بالعبادة مع اعتقادِ وحدته والتصديق بها ذاتاً وصفات وأفعالاً ; فليس هناك ذات تشبه ذاته تعالى ولا تقبل ذاته الانقسام لا فعلاً ولا وهماً ولا فرضاً مطابقاً للواقع (3) ، ولا تشبه صفاته الصفاتِ، ولا تعدُّدَ فيها من جنس واحد; بأن يكون له تعالى قدرتان مثلاً ، ولا يدخل أفعاله الاشتراك ; إذ لا فعل لغيره سبحانه وتعالى خلقاً وإن نُسب إلى غيره كسباً (4) وقيل (5) : هو إثباتُ ذاتٍ غير مشبِهَةٍ للذواتِ ولا معطلة عن الصفات، خلافاً للمعتزلة المعطلين للذات عن الصفات الوجودية (6) .
And what is intended here is the legal (shar‘ī) meaning, not in the sense of the codified discipline that will come later. And it (i.e., legal tawhīd) is: singling out the worshipped One with worship, along with belief in His oneness and affirmation of it in essence, attributes, and actions; so there is no essence that resembles His essence, nor does His essence accept division actually, hypothetically, or by a supposition that conforms to reality; nor do His attributes resemble [other] attributes, nor is there multiplicity among them of one kind, such as His having, for example, two powers; and actions cannot be shared; for there is no action for other than Him, glory be to Him, in terms of creation, even if it is attributed to others in terms of acquisition. And it is said: it is affirmation of an essence not resembling [other] essences and not stripped of attributes, contrary to the Mu‘tazila who strip the essence of existential attributes.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage provides the legal definition of tawhid, which is the intended meaning in the poem, as opposed to the technical definition of the science. The definition is: singling out the worshipped One with worship, combined with belief and affirmation in three categories: essence (dhāt), attributes (ṣifāt), and actions (af‘āl). The author then elaborates each: (1) Regarding essence: no essence resembles Allah's essence, and His essence does not accept division in any way—actual, hypothetical, or by a supposition that matches reality. (2) Regarding attributes: they do not resemble created attributes, and there is no multiplicity of the same kind (e.g., two powers). (3) Regarding actions: no one else creates any action; actions are attributed to humans only as acquisition (kasb), not creation (khalq). Finally, the author cites an alternative definition: affirmation of an essence that is neither resembling nor stripped of attributes, which refutes both the anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) and the Mu‘tazila who negate eternal attributes.

توحيد الصفات
توحيد الأفعال

Key Takeaway
The legal definition of tawhid includes affirming Allah's oneness in essence, attributes, and actions, while rejecting resemblance and negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage teaches that no human truly creates any action; even your own deeds are only acquired by you. Does this knowledge change how you view your successes and failures? When you do something good, do you attribute it to Allah as the Creator, or do you unconsciously feel proud as if you produced it? Commit to saying 'Alhamdulillah' with this deeper awareness.
?
The Muʿtazila stripped Allah of His attributes thinking they were preserving His oneness. But the correct tawhid affirms both oneness and attributes. Have you ever, in an attempt to avoid anthropomorphism, fallen into negating what Allah affirmed for Himself? This lesson is a warning. The Grammatical and Theological Meanings of 'al-Haqq'
Author explains the grammatical attachment and meaning of 'al-haqq' as a name of Allah or as truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical structure of the phrase 'lidini al-haqq' and the two possible meanings of 'al-haqq' in this context, and how each meaning affects the interpretation of the construct phrase.

Core Idea
The word 'al-haqq' can mean either Allah or the truth, changing the grammar of the phrase.

This short passage from the commentary unpacks a key phrase in the poem's opening line: 'lidini al-haqq'. The author first explains how the phrase is grammatically connected to the verb 'arshad', then examines the word 'al-haqq' itself, showing that it can be understood in two ways—as a name of Allah or as 'the truth'. Understanding this duality is essential for grasping the poem's theological precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's step-by-step method: he starts with grammar, then moves to lexical meaning, then ties it back to the construct phrase.
The passage is concise but packed with grammatical and theological insight.
2
Focus on the grammatical attachment
Note how the phrase 'lidini al-haqq' is attached to 'arshad'. The author discusses two possible interpretations of the preposition 'lām': as meaning 'concerning' (if irshad is interpreted as indication) or as meaning 'to' (if irshad is kept in its real sense).
This shows the flexibility of Arabic prepositional usage in theological texts.
3
Understand the two meanings of 'al-haqq'
Identify the two meanings: (1) a name of Allah, meaning the eternally existent One; (2) what corresponds to reality (the truth). The author says both are valid interpretations in this context.
The first meaning is primary, but the second is also possible and leads to a different grammatical analysis.
4
Learn how the idafa changes
See how the construct phrase 'dīn al-haqq' is interpreted: if 'al-haqq' means Allah, the idafa is possessive (the religion of Allah); if it means truth, the idafa is explanatory (the religion that is the true rulings).
This demonstrates that theological interpretation affects grammatical analysis.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters. The poem's phrase can be read as 'the religion of Allah' or 'the true religion'—both are theologically correct but emphasize different aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (لِدِينِ الحَقِّ) متعلق بـ (أرشد)، ومادة الإرشاد تتعدى باللام كما تتعدى ب (على)، فمن فسَّرَ الإرشاد بالدلالة فسَّر اللام بـ (على)، ومن أبقى الإرشاد على معناه الحقيقي، أبقى اللام على حقيقتها ؛ فإنه يقال : أرشدني لكذا. والمراد من (الحق) هنا : الله تعالى؛ لأنه اسم من أسمائه تعالى، ومعناه: المتحقق وجوده دائماً وأبداً، بحيث لا يسبقه عدم ولا يلحقه عدم، ويصح أن يراد بالحقِّ هنا ما طابقه الواقع، وإضافة الدين للحقِّ على الأول على معنى اللام، وعلى الثاني للبيان؛ أي: لدين هو الأحكام الحقَّة.
And His saying: 'for the religion of truth (lidīni al-ḥaqq)' is attached to 'guided' (arshad). The root of irshad is transitive with the lām as it is transitive with 'alā. So whoever interprets irshad as indication interprets the lām as 'alā, and whoever keeps irshad on its real meaning keeps the lām on its real meaning; for it is said: 'He guided me to such-and-such.' And what is meant by 'al-ḥaqq' here is Allah the Exalted, because it is one of His names, and its meaning is: He whose existence is realized constantly and eternally, such that no non-existence precedes Him nor non-existence follows Him. And it is valid that what is meant by 'al-ḥaqq' here is what corresponds to reality. And the idāfa of 'religion' to 'al-ḥaqq' according to the first is on the meaning of the lām, and according to the second is for bayān (clarification); i.e., the religion that is the true rulings.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological explanation of the phrase 'lidīni al-ḥaqq' (to the religion of truth). The author first notes that the phrase is attached (muta‘alliq) to the verb 'arshad' (guided). The verb 'arshada' can take a direct object via the preposition 'lām' or '‘alā'. If one interprets 'irshad' metaphorically as 'indication' (dalāla), then the lām is interpreted as '‘alā' (meaning 'to' or 'concerning'). If one keeps 'irshad' in its real sense (making someone guided), then the lām retains its real meaning of purpose or destination, as in the phrase 'arshadanī li-kadhā' (he guided me to such-and-such). The author then defines 'al-ḥaqq' in this context. Primarily, it is a name of Allah, meaning the Eternal Being who always exists, without beginning or end. However, it can also mean 'what corresponds to reality' (the truth). The idāfa (construct phrase) 'dīn al-ḥaqq' is interpreted differently depending on which meaning of 'al-ḥaqq' is taken: if it means Allah, then the idāfa is possessive (li-), i.e., the religion belonging to Allah. If it means the truth, then the idāfa is explanatory (bayān), i.e., the religion that consists of true rulings.

Key Terms
الحق
الإضافة اللامية
الإضافة البيانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'al-haqq' is only a name of Allah and cannot mean anything else in this context.
Correction: The author explicitly states that while the primary meaning is Allah, it is also valid to interpret 'al-haqq' as 'what corresponds to reality' (the truth), and this changes the grammatical analysis.

Checkpoint

C) The Prophet and the truth

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'lidini al-haqq' can be interpreted in two ways depending on whether 'al-haqq' refers to Allah or to truth, affecting the grammar and theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author explains that 'al-Haqq' as Allah's name means 'He whose existence is realized constantly and eternally, such that no non-existence precedes Him nor follows Him.' If not, what specific habit or thought pattern prevents you from feeling that reality?

Three Answers to the Objection of Excluding Prophets
Three answers to an objection about the author's wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three reasons why the author of Jawharat al-Tawhid only mentioned 'messengers' and not 'prophets' in the line about the Seal.

Core Idea
The author's choice of 'messengers' is intentional and defended by three scholarly answers.

When studying the poem, one might wonder why the author says 'seal of the messengers' instead of 'seal of the prophets.' This objection is raised and answered with three distinct responses, each drawing on different principles of Arabic rhetoric and theology. In this lesson, we will examine these answers and the deeper discussion about the relative merits of prophethood and messengership.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and three answers
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by three numbered answers.
The objection is based on the logical point that sealing the specific (messengers) does not imply sealing the general (prophets).
2
Understand the first answer: metonymy
The first answer says 'messengers' is used as a metonymy for 'prophets'. Identify the rhetorical term 'majaz mursal' and note that it allows using the specific for the general.
3
Understand the second answer: sufficiency (iktifa')
The second answer uses the principle of iktifa' (sufficiency) to imply that 'prophets' is understood from context, citing the Quranic example of 'shirts that protect you from heat' meaning also from cold.
4
Examine the third answer: equality and praiseworthiness
The third answer, from al-Mallawi, builds on al-Sa'd's view that messenger and prophet are equal, but argues that 'messenger' is more praiseworthy because it combines both the Divine and creation. Contrast this with al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam's view that prophethood is superior.
The quote from Qawa'id al-Ahkam supports the view that prophethood is superior, but the refutation is that messengership combines both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: كما أنه خاتم الرسل هو خاتم للأنبياء، فلم اقتصر المصنف على الأول مع أنه لا يلزم من ختمه للرسل ختمُهُ للأنبياء؛ إذ لا يلزم من ختم الأخص ختم الأعم؟ أجيب بثلاثة أجوبة : الأول: أن المراد بالرسل الأنبياء، فقد أطلق الخاص وأراد العام مجازاً مرسلاً . الثاني : أن في الكلام اكتفاء، والتقدير : لرسل ربه وأنبيائه ؛ على حد قوله تعالى : سَرَابِيلَ تَقِيكُمُ الْحَرَّ [النحل: ۸۱] أي: والبرد. الثالث : ما قاله الشيخ الملوي من حمله على ما تقدم عن السعد من تساوي الرسول والنبي، وإنما اختار التعبير بالرسل لأنه أمدح ؛ فإن الرسالة أشرفُ من النبوة؛ لجمعها بين الحقِّ والخلق، خلافاً للعزّ بن عبد السلام في قوله بأن النبوة أفضلُ؛ معللاً بأن فيها الانصراف من حضرة الخلق إلى الحقِّ، والرسالة فيها الانصراف من حضرة الحقِّ إلى الخلق ، ورُدَّ بأن الرسالة فيها الجمع بينهما كما علمت . وعبارته في قواعد الأحكام (۲۳۶/٢) : (النبوة أفضل ؛ لأن النبوة إخبارٌ عمَّا يستحقه الربُّ من صفات الجمال ونعوت الكمال، وهي متعلقة بالله من طرفيها، والإرسال دونَها ، أمر بالإبلاغ إلى العباد، فهو متعلق بالله من أحد طرفيه، وبالعباد من الطرف الآخر، ولا شك أن ما يتعلَّقُ من طرقيه أفضلُ ممَّا يتعلَّق به من أحد طرفيه، والنبوة سابقة على الإرسال ؛ فإن قول الله لموسى : إنّت أَنَا اللهُ رَبُّ الْعَالَمِينَ ﴾ [القصص : ۳۰] مقدَّم على قوله : اذْهَبْ إِلَى فِرْعَوْنَ إِنَّهُ طَغَى [النازعات: ١٧]، فجميع ما تحدَّثَ به قبل قوله : اذهب إلى فرعون .. نبوة، وما أمره بعد ذلك من التبليغ فهو إرسال).
If it is said: Just as he is the seal of messengers, he is also seal of prophets. So why did the author restrict it to the first, whereas it does not follow from his being seal of messengers that he is seal of prophets, since it does not follow from sealing the more specific that he seals the more general? He answered with three answers: First: That by 'messengers' he meant prophets, so he used the specific intending the general as a metonymy (majaz mursal). Second: That in the speech there is sufficiency (iktifa'), and the implied meaning is 'to the messengers of his Lord and his prophets', analogous to His saying: 'Shirts that protect you from the heat' (Quran 16:81), i.e., and from the cold. Third: What al-Mallawi said: that it is based on what al-Sa'd mentioned about the equality of messenger and prophet, and he only chose the expression 'messengers' because it is more praiseworthy; for indeed messengership (risala) is nobler than prophethood (nubuwwa), because it combines between the Right (haqq) and creation (khalq), contrary to al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam in his saying that prophethood is better, reasoning that in it there is turning away from the presence of creation to the Right, and in messengership there is turning away from the presence of the Right to creation. And it was refuted that in messengership there is combining both, as you know. And his phrase in Qawa'id al-Ahkam (2/236): 'Prophethood is better, because prophethood is informing about what the Lord deserves of attributes of beauty and qualities of perfection, and it is related to Allah from both its ends, while sending (irsal) is below it, being a command to convey to servants, so it is related to Allah from one of its ends and to servants from the other end. There is no doubt that what is related from its two ends is better than what is related from one of its ends. And prophethood precedes sending; for indeed Allah's saying to Moses: "Indeed I am Allah, Lord of the worlds" (Quran 28:30) precedes His saying: "Go to Pharaoh, indeed he has transgressed" (Quran 79:17). So all that he spoke before His saying "Go to Pharaoh" is prophethood, and what He commanded him after that of conveying is sending.'
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer with three enumerated responses. The objection argues that since the Prophet is seal of both messengers and prophets, the author should have mentioned prophets. The first answer says 'messengers' is a metonymy (majaz mursal) for 'prophets'. The second uses the principle of iktifa' (sufficiency) to imply both from context, citing the Quranic example. The third, attributed to al-Mallawi, relies on the equality of messenger and prophet according to al-Sa'd, but notes that 'messengers' is more praiseworthy because risala combines both the Divine and creation, whereas prophethood is only related to the Divine. This is contrasted with al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam's view that prophethood is superior, which is then refuted. The commentator includes a supporting quote from Qawa'id al-Ahkam elaborating on the superiority of prophethood based on its exclusive focus on Allah, but this seems to support al-Izz's view; however, the refutation is that risala combines both, and the quote may be presented as the opposing view. The argument is complex, emphasizing the honor of the term 'messenger'.

Key Terms
الرسالة
النبوة
المجاز المرسل
الاكتفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's omission of 'prophets' was an oversight or mistake.
Correction: The three answers show that the omission is intentional and justified through rhetorical principles and theological reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the third answer, why is الرسالة (messengership) more praiseworthy than النبوة (prophethood)?
A) Because it involves only the Right.
B) Because it combines both the Right and creation.
C) Because it turns from creation to the Right.
D) Because it is a more general term.

Key Takeaway
The three answers demonstrate that the author's choice of 'messengers' is rhetorically sound and theologically meaningful, reflecting a deliberate intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you refer to the Prophet ﷺ as the 'Seal of the Prophets', do you truly appreciate that his role as a messenger (rasul) combines the high honour of receiving from Allah with the duty of conveying to creation? How does this affect your sense of responsibility in conveying the message to others?
?
The author deliberately chose 'messengers' over 'prophets' because it is more praiseworthy. Are you ever tempted to use language that elevates yourself or others for worldly praise, rather than using precise words for the sake of truth? What does this teach you about sincerity in speech?
?
The third answer argues that risala is nobler because it includes both the Divine and creation. Does your understanding of the Prophet's mission change when you realize he is not only a recipient of revelation but also an active conveyor? How can you embody this combination in your own life—balancing devotion to Allah with service to His creation?

The Correct Derivation of 'Murabb' (The Nurturer)
Commentary on the word 'murabb' as an active participle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'murabb' must be interpreted as an active participle rather than a verbal noun to avoid a theologically problematic implication.

Core Idea
Calling Allah 'Murabb' means He is the active nurturer, not the act itself.

This lesson explores a subtle but important point of Arabic grammar and theology. The word 'murabb' appears in a poetic line as a name of Allah. Some scholars mistakenly took it as a verbal noun, implying Allah is the very act of upbringing. The commentator corrects this, showing it is an active participle, preserving Allah's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic line
First, read the line from the poem that lists divine attributes, including 'murabb'. Notice that it is presented among other names of Allah.
The line is a key part of the matn that the commentator explains.
2
Understand the mistaken view
Some scholars claimed 'murabb' is a verbal noun meaning 'tarbiyah' (upbringing) and applied it to Allah hyperbolically. Consider what that would imply.
3
Recognize the problem
The commentator calls this view 'ugly' because it would mean Allah is identical to the act of upbringing, which is anthropomorphic. Evaluate why this is theologically dangerous.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Learn the correct derivation
The correct view is that 'murabb' is an active participle (ism fa'il). It originally was 'raabib', then underwent phonetic changes: deletion of alif and assimilation of the two ba's (idgham).
This preserves the meaning that Allah is the One who nurtures, not the act itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قريب محيط مالك ومدبر مرب كثير الخير والمول للنعم وخالقنا المعبود جابر كسرنا ومصلحنا والصاحب الثابت القدم وجامعنا والسيد احفظ فهذه معان أتت للرب فادع لمن نظم. وقع في عبارة كثير من العلماء أنه مصدر بمعنى التربية؛ وهي تبليغ الشيء شيئاً فشيئاً إلى الحد الذي أراده المربي، أطلق عليه تعالى مبالغة؛ أي: بدعوى أنه تعالى هو عين التربية، ولا يخفى ما فيه من البشاعة؛ فالأولى أنه اسم فاعل، فأصله (رابب)، ثم خفف بحذف الألف وإدغام أحد المثلين في
Close, encompassing, owner, manager, nurturer (murabb), abundant in goodness, bestower of blessings, our Creator, the worshipped, mender of our brokenness, our reformer, the eternal associate, the gatherer, the Master. Preserve! So these meanings have come for the Lord (al-Rabb), so pray for the one who composed. And it occurs in the expression of many scholars that it (i.e., 'murabb') is a verbal noun (masdar) meaning tarbiyah (upbringing), which is delivering something gradually to the limit intended by the one raising. It is applied to Him, exalted, hyperbolically, i.e., claiming that He, exalted, is the very essence of tarbiyah. What is in this of ugliness is not hidden. So the preferable view is that it is an active participle (ism fa'il). Its original is 'raabib', then it was lightened by deleting the alif and assimilating one of the two identical letters in...
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the word 'murabb' (nurturer) appearing in the poetic line. The author first presents the line of the poem that includes the word 'murabb' as one of the names/attributes of Allah. Then he explains that many scholars claim 'murabb' is a verbal noun (masdar) meaning 'tarbiyah' (upbringing), with the connotation of bringing something to completion gradually. The word would then be applied to Allah hyperbolically, as if saying He is the very act of upbringing. The author criticizes this view as ugly because it implies anthropomorphism by treating a created act as an essence of Allah. Therefore, the correct view is that 'murabb' is an active participle (ism fa'il) derived from the verb 'rabbaba', with the original form 'raabib', which then underwent phonetic changes: elision of the alif and assimilation of one of the two identical letters (the two ba's). The argument follows the order: statement of the view, refutation of its implication, and proposal of a theologically sound alternative.

Key Terms

مرب
تربية
مصدر
اسم فاعل
رابب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Murabb is a verbal noun meaning the act of upbringing itself.
Correction: Murabb is an active participle meaning the One who nurtures, and interpreting it as a verbal noun leads to anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the view that 'murabb' is a verbal noun (masdar) problematic?
A) Because it requires the word to be formed from a weak root.
B) Because it implies that Allah is identical to the act of upbringing, which is anthropomorphic.
C) Because it contradicts the meter of the poem.
D) Because it is grammatically impossible.

Key Takeaway
The word 'murabb' must be understood as an active participle to avoid attributing a created act as Allah's essence.

Knowing that 'murabb' means He actively nurtures you, what area of your life do you need to surrender more fully to His nurturing? Write down one practical step to trust Him more in that area starting today.

The Meaning of Tama' and Thawab
Commentator explains the phrase and defines thawab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's clarification that 'tama'' in the poem means hope (raja') and the definition of thawab (reward) as a measure of recompense given by God's sheer choice, not by obligation.

Core Idea
Reward is a gift of pure grace, not an earned wage.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a phrase from the poem: 'fi al-thawabi tami'an' (in reward, hoping). He explains the grammatical construction and clarifies that 'tama'' here is figurative for hope, not greed. Then he defines thawab as a recompense known only to God, given by His choice alone. This sets the stage for rejecting any notion that God is obligated to reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview. Notice the grammatical terms like 'jar wa majrur' and 'sifah'.
2
Analyze the meaning of 'tama''
The commentator says 'tama'' here is metaphorical for 'raja'' (hope). Understand why a seeker of Allah's face is hopeful, not greedy.
The shift in terminology is important for avoiding misunderstanding.
3
Grasp the definition of thawab
Thawab is defined as a measure of recompense prepared by Allah for whom He wills, by His sheer choice, not by compulsion (ijab) or obligation (wujub). Note the upcoming verse reference.
This lays the foundation for the refutation of the philosophers in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فِي الثَّوابِ طَامِعاً) الجار والمجرور متعلق بما بعده، وقدمه عليه لضرورة النظم، و ( طامعاً) صفة لـ (مريداً)، ويصح أن يكون حالاً من فاعل (أرجو) أي: أرجو الله في القبول حال كوني طامعاً والمراد بالطمع هنا: الرجاء على سبيل التجوز؛ لأن من أراد هذه الأرجوزة وقصد بها وجه الله تعالى كان راجياً للثواب لا طامعاً . والثواب : مقدار من الجزاء يعلمُهُ الله تعالى أعدَّهُ لمن شاء من عباده في نظير أعمالهم الحسنة بمحض اختياره لا بالإيجاب ولا بالوجوب كما سيأتي التصريح به في قوله : فإن يُثنبا فَبِمحض الفَضلِ. وفي قولنا : (لا) (بالإيجاب ردّ على الفلاسفة القائلين بالإيجاب؛ أي: التعليل؛ بمعنى أن الثواب ينشأ عن ذات الله قهراً كحركة الخاتم؛ فإنهم يقولون: إنها تنشأ عن حركة الإصبع بطريق التعليل.
His saying: 'in reward, hoping' (fi al-thawabi tami'an) — the preposition and its object are attached to what follows, and it is fronted due to poetic necessity. 'Hoping' (tami'an) is an adjective for 'wishing' (mureedan), and it may be a circumstantial accusative from the subject of 'arju', i.e., 'I hope for God concerning acceptance while I am hoping.' What is meant by 'hope' (tama') here is 'expectation' (raja') metaphorically, because whoever intends this urjuza and aims by it for the face of God is a raji (hopeful) for reward, not a tami' (greedy). Reward (thawab) is a measure of recompense known to God, prepared by Him for whomever He wills among His servants in return for their good deeds, by His sheer choice, not by compulsion (ijab) nor by obligation (wujub), as will be explicitly stated in his words: 'For if He rewards us, it is by pure grace.' Our saying 'not by compulsion' is a refutation of the philosophers who assert compulsion (ijab), i.e., necessitation, meaning that reward emanates from God's essence necessarily, like the movement of a ring; for they say it emanates from the movement of the finger by way of necessitation.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'fi al-thawabi tami'an' (in reward, hoping). The commentator states that the prepositional phrase is fronted for poetic meter. 'Tami'an' is an adjective for 'mureedan' or a circumstantial accusative from the subject of 'arju'. He clarifies that 'tama'' here is figurative for 'raja'' (hope), because one who seeks the urjuza for God's sake hopes for reward, not greedily. Then he defines 'thawab' (reward) as a measure of recompense known to God, given to whomever He wills, by His sheer choice, not by compulsion or obligation. This is a refutation of the philosophers who claim that reward necessarily emanates from God's essence like a necessary effect from its cause (e.g., the ring's movement from the finger). The commentator asserts that reward is purely a matter of divine grace, not necessitation.

Refutation of Philosophers on Divine Necessitation
Refutation of philosophers on necessitation of reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the philosophers who claim that reward necessarily emanates from Allah's essence, like a natural effect from its cause.

Core Idea
God's reward is voluntary, not necessitated like a physical effect.

The commentator now addresses a serious philosophical error: the claim that reward flows from Allah by necessity, just as a ring moves inevitably when the finger moves. This lesson explains why such necessitation (ijab) is false and that reward is purely an act of grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to understand the refutation. Note the key term 'ijab' (necessitation) and the analogy of the ring.
2
Understand the philosophers' position
The philosophers assert that reward emanates from Allah's essence necessarily, similar to a ring's movement following the finger's movement by natural causation.
This view eliminates divine will and choice.
3
Recognize the refutation
The commentator refutes this by referencing the earlier definition: reward is by pure grace, not by compulsion. The author's statement 'by pure grace' directly contradicts the necessitation view.
The refutation is implicit but clear.

Reward: God's Pure Grace, Not Necessity or Obligation
The commentator defines thawāb and ṭama‘ and refutes necessitarian and obligatorist views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘arī definition of reward (thawāb) as a voluntary gift from God, and the refutation of both the philosophers' necessitarianism and the Mu'tazila's claim of obligation.

Core Idea
Reward is a gift from God, never a right.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Murīd clarifies a central Ash‘arī conviction: that God rewards whom He wills by His sheer generosity, not because He is compelled. The commentator defines the terms ‘thawāb’ and ‘ṭama‘’ and then counters two opposing groups—the philosophers who see reward as a necessary emanation, and the Mu‘tazila who see it as an obligation. Understanding this sets the framework for the classical debate on divine justice and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the text first without analyzing; let the commentator's flow establish the key points.
Notice the careful definitions.
2
Understand the definition of thawāb
Identify the phrases that emphasize reward as a gift prepared by God's pure choice, not by necessity or obligation.
Compare with implicit opposing views.
3
Examine the refutation of the philosophers
The commentator rejects their view that reward arises necessarily from God's essence, using the analogy of a ring moving with a finger. Note the objection about resurrection and the answer.
The objection shows philosophers still affirm spiritual reward.
4
Examine the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila claim that what is most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) is obligatory upon God. The commentator rejects this and points to a later line for further refutation.
This preserves God's absolute freedom.
5
Reflect on the lesson's implications
Consider how the Ash‘arī view safeguards divine sovereignty while still affirming the reality of reward.
This connects to broader debates on divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بالثواب والطمع
في الثواب .
قوله : (فِي الثَّوابِ طَامِعاً) الجار والمجرور متعلق بما بعده، وقدمه عليه لضرورة النظم ، و ( طامعاً) صفة لـ (مريداً)، ويصح أن يكون حالاً من فاعل (أرجو) أي: أرجو الله في القبول حال كوني طامعاً والمراد بالطمع هنا: الرجاء على سبيل التجوز; لأن من أراد هذه الأرجوزة وقصد بها وجه الله تعالى كان راجياً للثواب لا طامعاً .
والثواب : مقدار من الجزاء يعلمُهُ الله تعالى أعدَّهُ لمن شاء من عباده في نظير أعمالهم الحسنة بمحض اختياره لا بالإيجاب ولا بالوجوب كما سيأتي التصريح به في قوله :
فإن يُثبنا فَبِمحض الفَضلِ
وفي قولنا : (لا) (بالإيجاب ردّ على الفلاسفة القائلين بالإيجاب; أي: التعليل; بمعنى أن الثواب ينشأ عن ذات الله قهراً كحركة الخاتم; فإنهم يقولون: إنها تنشأ عن حركة الإصبع بطريق التعليل.
فإن قيل : إن الفلاسفة ينكرون الحشر من أصله فلا يثبتون ثواباً لا بالإيجاب ولا بالوجوب 
أجيب بأنهم وإن أنكروا حشر الأجسام يقولون بحشر الأرواح، وتثاب باللذات المعنوية .
وفي قولنا : (ولا) بالوجوب) ردُّ على المعتزلة القائلين بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح; وسيأتي الرد عليهم بقوله : وقولهم: إنَّ الصلاح واجب علیه زور ما عليه واجب
The intended meaning of reward and hope. In [His saying]: 'in reward, hoping' (fī al-thawābi tāmi‘an), the prepositional phrase depends on what follows, and it was advanced due to metrical necessity. 'Hoping' (tāmi‘an) is an attribute of 'intending' (murīdan), and it can be a circumstantial accusative from the agent of 'I hope' – i.e. 'I hope in God for acceptance while I am hoping'. The intended meaning of 'tama‘' here is hope, metaphorically, because whoever intends this poem and aims thereby for God's face is hoping for reward, not covetous. Al-thawāb (reward) is a measure of recompense known to God, the Exalted, which He has prepared for whomever He wills among His servants in return for their good deeds, by His pure choice, not by necessitation (ījāb) nor by obligation (wujūb) – as will be explicitly stated in his line: 'For if He rewards us, it is by pure favor.' And in our statement: '(lā) (the negation of) necessitation (ījāb)', it is a refutation of the philosophers who hold necessitation (ījāb), i.e. causal emanation ('ilal), meaning that reward arises from God's essence necessarily like the movement of a ring from the movement of a finger. If it is said: The philosophers deny the resurrection (ḥashr) entirely, so they do not affirm reward whether by necessitation or obligation. The answer: Although they deny the resurrection of bodies, they affirm the resurrection of spirits, and they are rewarded with spiritual pleasures. And in our statement: '(walā) (and not) by obligation (wujūb)', it is a refutation of the Mu'tazila who hold the obligation of what is beneficial and most beneficial (ṣalāḥ wa aṣlaḥ). And the refutation of them will come in his line: 'And their statement that what is beneficial is obligatory upon Him is false; nothing is obligatory upon Him.'
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms 'thawāb' and 'tama‘' as used in the poem, emphasizing that 'tama‘' here means hope, not greed. The definition of reward (thawāb) is explicitly given as a voluntary gift from God, not necessitated or obligatory, contra the philosophers (who hold that reward emanates necessarily from God's essence) and the Mu'tazila (who hold that God must do what is best). Two refutations: (1) the philosophers' view of necessary emanation is rejected; (2) even though philosophers deny bodily resurrection, they affirm spiritual reward, so the objection fails. The Mu'tazila's view of obligation is similarly rejected, with a promise of further refutation.

Correction: Reward is a gift from God's pure favor, not an obligation.

Key Takeaway
Reward (thawāb) is a gift from Allah by His pure favor, not a necessity emanating from His essence nor an obligation upon Him.

The Three Divisions of Divine Attributes
Classification of divine attributes by evidence type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of Allah's attributes based on the type of evidence required to prove them and the rationale for the standard order of discussion.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are divided by how we know them: reason, revelation, or both.

The author explains that not all divine attributes are known in the same way. Some can only be proven through rational argument, others only through scripture, and one—unity—is debated. This classification helps us see why certain attributes are discussed first and why some require transmitted proof. As you read the passage, pay attention to the examples given for each category and the reasoning behind the order of necessary, impossible, and possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to get a clear sense of the author's classification.
Notice the footnote marker (1) which is retained from the original.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage presents three divisions of attributes. Find each one and note the examples given: first division (rational proof), second division (transmitted proof), third division (unity, debated).
The first division includes attributes on which the miracle depends; the second includes those on which it does not.
3
Understand the ordering rationale
The author explains why the necessary (wājib) is presented first, the impossible (muḥāl) last, and the possible (jāʾiz) in the middle. Relate this to the concepts of affirmation and negation.
Affirmation is nobler than negation, so the necessary (affirmation) comes first.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that all attributes are known by reason alone. This classification shows that some attributes require transmitted proof.
This reinforces the importance of both reason and revelation in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : (غالباً) لأن الصفاتِ على ثلاثة أقسام: القسم الأول: ما لا يصح الاستدلال عليه إلا بالدليل العقلي : وهو (۱) ما توقفت عليه المعجزة من الصفاتِ كوجوده تعالى وقدمه وبقائه وقيامه بنفسه ومخالفته للحوادث، وقدرته وإرادته وعلمه وحياته. القسم الثاني: ما لا يصح الاستدلال عليه إلا بالدليل السمعي : وهو كل ما لا تتوقَّفُ المعجزة عليه من الصفات كالسمع والبصر والكلام. القسم الثالث: ما اختلف فيه وهو الوحدانية : والأصح أن دليلها عقلي. وإنما قدَّمَ الواجب لشرفه، وأخَّرَ المستحيل لانحطاطِهِ؛ لأنه يرجع للسل والثبوتُ أشرفُ منه ؛ ووسط الجائز؛ لأن فيه شائبة الثبوت وشائبة السلب.
We say 'mostly' because attributes are of three divisions: The first division: that which it is not valid to infer except by rational proof: and it is (1) what the miracle depends on of attributes, such as His existence, may He be exalted, His pre-eternity, His everlastingness, His self-subsistence, His dissimilarity to contingent things, His power, His will, His knowledge, and His life. The second division: that which it is not valid to infer except by transmitted proof: and it is everything on which the miracle does not depend of attributes, such as hearing, sight, and speech. The third division: that concerning which there is disagreement, and it is unity: and the correct view is that its proof is rational. And he [the author] presented the necessary due to its honor, and delayed the impossible due to its lowliness; because it returns to negation, and affirmation is nobler than it; and he placed the possible in the middle because it contains a tinge of affirmation and a tinge of negation.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the attributes of Allah according to the type of evidence needed to prove them. The author first notes that his earlier phrase 'mostly' refers to this threefold division. The first division comprises attributes that can only be inferred through rational proof; these are the attributes on which the possibility of a miracle depends—such as existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity to contingents, power, will, knowledge, and life. The second division includes attributes that can only be inferred through transmitted (scriptural) proof; these are attributes on which the miracle does not depend, such as hearing, sight, and speech. The third division is unity (tawḥīd), about which there is disagreement; the correct position is that its proof is rational. The author then explains the reasoning behind the typical order of discussion: the necessary (wājib) is presented first because it is honorable, the impossible (muḥāl) is delayed because it is lowly (since it involves negation, and affirmation is nobler), and the possible (jāʾiz) is placed in the middle because it partakes of both affirmation and negation.

Key Terms
الصفات
الدليل العقلي
الدليل السمعي
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes can be proven by reason alone.
Correction: Some attributes, like hearing, sight, and speech, require transmitted proof (scripture) because they do not have a rational basis on which the miracle depends.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which attributes belong to the first division (known only by rational proof)?
A) Hearing, sight, and speech
B) Existence, pre-eternity, power, will, knowledge, and life
C) Unity (tawhid)
D) All attributes are known by transmitted proof

Key Takeaway
Attributes are not all known by the same method; some require rational proof, some require scriptural proof, and the order of discussion reflects the nobility of affirmation over negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you tend to rely more on what makes sense to your reason or on what you have been taught from scripture? How does this classification challenge or confirm your tendency?
?
The author prioritises affirmation over negation. Is there an area in your life where you focus more on what Allah is not (e.g., not unjust, not weak) rather than on what He is? How can you shift your mindset to affirm His perfect attributes first?
?
The unity of Allah (tawḥīd) is presented as something that reason can prove. Have you ever tried to rationally convince yourself of tawḥīd, or do you take it on faith alone? What would it take for you to be personally certain of tawḥīd through reason?

Ghazali's Statement on the Best Possible World
The author explains Ghazali's statement and gives the best defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true meaning of Ghazali's statement 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been' and the strongest answer to the objection that it implies God's incapacity.

Core Idea
Ghazali's statement refers to impossibility relative to God's decree, not absolute impossibility.

Imam al-Ghazali famously said 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been,' which stirred controversy. Critics claimed it limits God's power. In this passage, the author presents the best answer: the statement acknowledges that given God's eternal knowledge and will, the actual world is the one decreed, and any alternative is impossible in that sense—not because God is weak, but because it would contradict His prior determination.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's original statement
Read the Arabic and translation of the controversial phrase. Notice how it is phrased and why it might be misunderstood.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objection raised against Ghazali: that his statement implies God is incapable of creating a better world. Reflect on why this would be a serious charge.
3
Study the best answer
Read the best answer given in the passage. Note how it distinguishes between absolute possibility and possibility relative to God's will and knowledge.
The key is that impossibility here is due to the non-attachment of divine will and knowledge, not due to lack of power.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider the difference between 'possible in itself' and 'impossible because God did not will it.' This is the core insight that resolves the controversy.

Explanation of the meaning of our Imam al-Ghazali's statement: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been.' And it occurred in the words of al-Ghazali: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been.' So a group criticized him for it, claiming that it implies attributing incapacity to Allah, Exalted is He. And it was answered with several answers; the best of them is that the meaning is: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been' because the knowledge and will of Allah are not attached to anything other than what has been, which is the world. So it is impossible due to the non-attachment of Allah's knowledge and will to it. Thus, it is true that there is nothing more excellent in possibility in this respect, even though it is possible in itself.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage addresses a controversial statement by Imam al-Ghazali: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been' (ليس في الإمكان أبدع مما كان). Critics accused him of implying that God is incapable of creating a better world. The author presents the strongest answer: the statement means that given God's eternal knowledge and will, the world that actually exists is the one that was decreed. Any other possible world is not an object of God's will and knowledge, so it is impossible in that sense—not because God lacks power, but because it would contradict His prior determination. Thus, the statement refers to impossibility relative to God's decree, not absolute impossibility. The world as it is, is the best possible under the divine plan.

الأبدع
الإرادة
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali meant that God could not create a better world because He lacks power.
Correction: The impossibility is due to the non-attachment of God's will and knowledge to any other world, not due to any deficiency in power.

Checkpoint

A) The world could have been better but God chose not to
B) The statement means that other worlds are impossible because God's will and knowledge are not attached to them
C) Ghazali was misquoted
D) The world is the worst possible world

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's statement, properly understood, affirms that the actual world is the one decreed by God's wisdom; any alternative is impossible relative to that decree, not absolutely.

Defending Ghazali: The Best Answer to the Objection of Attributing Inability to God
Ghazali's statement, the objection, and the best answer with footnotes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the best answer to the objection that Ghazali's statement attributes inability to God, and the distinction between impossibility and inability.

Core Idea
The impossibility of a better world stems from divine will, not divine weakness.

Imam al-Ghazali's statement 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been' sparked controversy, with some accusing it of implying God could not create a better world. The best answer clarifies that the impossibility is due to the non-attachment of God's knowledge and will—a matter of impossibility, not inability. This lesson explains that key distinction.

Guided Path
1

The passage includes two footnotes; note how they support the main argument.

The Divine Matters: Explanation of the meaning of our Imam al-Ghazali's statement: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been.' And it occurred in al-Ghazali's speech: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been' (1), so a group criticized him, accusing it (2) of attributing inability to the Exalted. And it was answered with several answers; the best of them: that the meaning is: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been' due to the non-attachment of God's knowledge and will to other than what has been, which is the world; thus it is impossible because God's knowledge and will are not attached to it, so it is true that it is not in possibility in this respect, even if it is possible in itself. (1) This resounding phrase, which is repeated and said as it was said here, its origin is in Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din (4/258): in its entirety and its context with the wording: 'And everything that God the Exalted apportioned among His servants of provision and term, and joy and sorrow, and incapacity and ability, and faith and disbelief, and obedience and disobedience, all of it is pure justice with no injustice in it, and pure truth with no wrong in it; rather it is upon the obligatory order of truth, as it should be, and as it should be, and in the amount that it should be; and there is nothing in possibility at all more beautiful than it, nor more complete, nor more perfect; and if there were and He withheld it while having the ability and did not do it, it would be miserliness contradicting generosity, and injustice contradicting justice; and if He were not able, it would be inability contradicting divinity.' (2) His statement: 'that it contains... etc.' – it does not appear from al-Ghazali's speech any attribution of inability at all; because he only denied possibility, so he is saying that God's power does not relate to creation due to its impossibility, and there is no attribution of inability in this, as is not hidden. So the better objection against him is to say: he denied the possibility of creation; and the reality is that it is possible in itself, meaning that it is in itself suitable for existence and non-existence. End of al-Ajhuri. And this interpretation perhaps came from the statement of the Imam at the conclusion of his discussion of this meaning: (The summary: good and evil are decreed, and what has been decreed becomes necessary after the precedence of the will, so there is no repeller of His judgment, nor any reviewer of His decree and command; rather every small and great is recorded, and its occurrence is by a known measured destiny; and what befalls you was not to miss you, and what misses you was not to befall you). But we are almost certain that the intended meaning of this statement according to our Imam al-Ghazali is not only this; rather it is a direction, and the worst answer to this statement is that it was said to be placed upon him, since he mentioned it more than once in the Book of Tawhid and Tawakkul from his Ihya'! Even al-Hafiz al-Zabidi in his Ithaf (9/430) commented on it with his statement: 'Thus is the text of the phrase in all copies of the book, especially in some of their endings that it was transmitted from a reliable copy, trusted for its correctness.' Rather the better answer is to say: all events are in one rank, they never leave it; indeed the power of the Powerful does not at all relate to taking them out of it; and it is the rank of origination (huduth); they are equal in this between the wondrous (badi') and the more wondrous (abda'); rather all creation is in the rank of the more wondrous according to the verifiers. And whoever knows the Truth Exalted and that He is Wise and Generous, then after that supposes that He withheld from =
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on Imam al-Ghazali's famous statement, 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been.' The commentary first notes that a group criticized this statement, accusing it of attributing inability to God—implying that God could have created something better but did not. The best answer is then given: the intended meaning is that nothing more excellent is possible because God's knowledge and will are not attached to anything other than what actually exists. Therefore, any supposed 'better' world is impossible in that respect, even if it is possible in itself. The commentary includes two footnotes: the first traces the original context in Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din, where al-Ghazali argues that everything is just and perfect; the second (from al-Ajhuri) clarifies that al-Ghazali's statement does not imply inability—he only denies possibility, which is a different matter. The commentary then discusses an interpretation from the Imam's own conclusion about divine decree, and critiques those who reject the statement, noting that al-Zabidi confirmed its authenticity. Finally, it presents the 'better answer': all events are in the single rank of origination (huduth), so they are all equally 'more wondrous' (abda'). The passage ends abruptly at the page break, likely continuing the argument.

الأبدع
تعلق العلم
