العجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali's statement means God could not create a better world because He lacks power.
Correction: The statement means that a better world is impossible because God's knowledge and will are not attached to it; it is a matter of impossibility, not inability.

Checkpoint
What is the 'best answer' given in the commentary to the objection that Ghazali's statement attributes inability to God?
A) The statement means God cannot create anything better because He is not powerful enough.
B) The statement means that nothing more excellent is possible because God's knowledge and will are not attached to anything else.
C) The statement is a metaphor and not to be taken literally.
D) The statement was incorrectly attributed to Ghazali.

Key Takeaway
The best answer to the objection is that Ghazali's statement does not deny God's power but affirms that nothing more excellent is possible because God's will is not attached to anything else.

The Ultimate Reconciliation: All Creation Equally Perfect
Interpretation of Ghazali's statement, authenticity debate, and final reconciliation via the rank of huduth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final argument that all events are in the same rank of origination (huduth), making them equally 'more wondrous' and resolving the apparent issue with Ghazali's statement.

Core Idea
All creation is equally perfect because everything is in the rank of huduth.

After addressing the initial objection, the commentary presents a deeper reconciliation: all events are in the single rank of origination (huduth). This means that no created thing can be 'more excellent' than another in a way that would challenge Ghazali's statement. The lesson also touches on the authenticity of the statement and the error of those who reject it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the second part of the source, focusing on the interpretation from the Imam's conclusion, the critique of rejecters, and the final answer about huduth.
The passage is cut off at the end of the chunk; it likely continues the argument.
2
Understand the interpretation from the Imam's conclusion
The passage references a conclusion about divine decree: good and evil are decreed and become necessary. This suggests that Ghazali's statement fits into a broader framework of predestination.
The phrase 'والحاصل: أن الخير والشر مقضي به' is key.
3
Recognize the authenticity debate
Some have claimed the statement is inauthentic, but the commentary notes that Ghazali mentioned it multiple times, and al-Hafiz al-Zabidi confirmed its correctness in all copies of Ihya'.
Al-Zabidi's comment: 'هكذا نص العبارة في سائر نسخ الكتاب'.
4
Grasp the final answer: the rank of huduth
The best answer is that all events are in a single rank—the rank of origination (huduth). Therefore, they are all equally 'more wondrous' (abda'). There is no room for one being more or less excellent.
The phrase 'الحوادث كلها في رتبة واحدة' is central.

The Divine Matters: Explanation of the meaning of our Imam al-Ghazali's statement: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been.' And it occurred in al-Ghazali's speech: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been' (1), so a group criticized him, accusing it (2) of attributing inability to the Exalted. And it was answered with several answers; the best of them: that the meaning is: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been' due to the non-attachment of God's knowledge and will to other than what has been, which is the world; thus it is impossible because God's knowledge and will are not attached to it, so it is true that it is not in possibility in this respect, even if it is possible in itself. (1) This resounding phrase, which is repeated and said as it was said here, its origin is in Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din (4/258): in its entirety and its context with the wording: 'And everything that God the Exalted apportioned among His servants of provision and term, and joy and sorrow, and incapacity and ability, and faith and disbelief, and obedience and disobedience, all of it is pure justice with no injustice in it, and pure truth with no wrong in it; rather it is upon the obligatory order of truth, as it should be, and as it should be, and in the amount that it should be; and there is nothing in possibility at all more beautiful than it, nor more complete, nor more perfect; and if there were and He withheld it while having the ability and did not do it, it would be miserliness contradicting generosity, and injustice contradicting justice; and if He were not able, it would be inability contradicting divinity.' (2) His statement: 'that it contains... etc.' – it does not appear from al-Ghazali's speech any attribution of inability at all; because he only denied possibility, so he is saying that God's power does not relate to creation due to its impossibility, and there is no attribution of inability in this, as is not hidden. So the better objection against him is to say: he denied the possibility of creation; and the reality is that it is possible in itself, meaning that it is in itself suitable for existence and non-existence. End of al-Ajhuri. And this interpretation perhaps came from the statement of the Imam at the conclusion of his discussion of this meaning: (The summary: good and evil are decreed, and what has been decreed becomes necessary after the precedence of the will, so there is no repeller of His judgment, nor any reviewer of His decree and command; rather every small and great is recorded, and its occurrence is by a known measured destiny; and what befalls you was not to miss you, and what misses you was not to befall you). But we are almost certain that the intended meaning of this statement according to our Imam al-Ghazali is not only this; rather it is a direction, and the worst answer to this statement is that it was said to be placed upon him, since he mentioned it more than once in the Book of Tawhid and Tawakkul from his Ihya'! Even al-Hafiz al-Zabidi in his Ithaf (9/430) commented on it with his statement: 'Thus is the text of the phrase in all copies of the book, especially in some of their endings that it was transmitted from a reliable copy, trusted for its correctness.' Rather the better answer is to say: all events are in one rank, they never leave it; indeed the power of the Powerful does not at all relate to taking them out of it; and it is the rank of origination (huduth); they are equal in this between the wondrous (badi') and the more wondrous (abda'); rather all creation is in the rank of the more wondrous according to the verifiers. And whoever knows the Truth Exalted and that He is Wise and Generous, then after that supposes that He withheld from =
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on Imam al-Ghazali's famous statement, 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has been.' The commentary first notes that a group criticized this statement, accusing it of attributing inability to God—implying that God could have created something better but did not. The best answer is then given: the intended meaning is that nothing more excellent is possible because God's knowledge and will are not attached to anything other than what actually exists. Therefore, any supposed 'better' world is impossible in that respect, even if it is possible in itself. The commentary includes two footnotes: the first traces the original context in Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din, where al-Ghazali argues that everything is just and perfect; the second (from al-Ajhuri) clarifies that al-Ghazali's statement does not imply inability—he only denies possibility, which is a different matter. The commentary then discusses an interpretation from the Imam's own conclusion about divine decree, and critiques those who reject the statement, noting that al-Zabidi confirmed its authenticity. Finally, it presents the 'better answer': all events are in the single rank of origination (huduth), so they are all equally 'more wondrous' (abda'). The passage ends abruptly at the page break, likely continuing the argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some creations are more perfect than others, so God could have created a better world.
Correction: All creations are in the same rank of origination (huduth), making them equally 'more wondrous.' The concept of 'better' does not apply within this rank.

Checkpoint
According to the final better answer, why are all events equally 'more wondrous' (abda')?
A) Because God is all-powerful and can only create perfect things.
B) Because they are all in the same rank of origination (huduth).
C) Because al-Ghazali said so in his book.
D) Because they are all good.

Key Takeaway
The final reconciliation is that all events are in the same rank of origination, making the notion of 'more excellent' irrelevant—everything is equally perfect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that everything in your life—both the seemingly good and the seemingly bad—is equally in the rank of huduth, and therefore equally 'more wondrous'? How does that challenge your dissatisfaction with what Allah has decreed for you? Write one area where you need to accept this truth and change your complaint into gratitude.
?
If Allah's power does not extend to making something 'more excellent' than what He did because all is in the same rank, what then of your own judgments of what is better or worse in your life? Will you submit to His wisdom and generosity, even when you don't see it? Commit to one specific situation where you will stop comparing and start praising.
?
Al-Ghazali's statement was defended by verifying its authenticity and by a deep philosophical insight. But do you really feel the weight of the claim that all creation is equally perfect? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that? What resentment or pride would it dissolve? Give a concrete answer.

The Relative Nature of Good and Evil: A Theodicy
Commentary theodicy: all worldly harms are relative and lead to greater good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of evil and deficiency does not contradict divine wisdom, and how opposites allow appreciation of perfection.

Core Idea
All worldly harms are relative and serve to highlight greater goods in the Hereafter.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Murid explains that what appears as evil or deficiency in this world is actually a means of increasing reward in the Hereafter. The commentator argues that without opposites—like night and day, illness and health—the value of blessings would not be known. This understanding resolves the apparent problem of evil in a world created by a perfectly wise God.

Guided Path
1
Read the theodicy argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the examples (poverty, illness, fire) that illustrate how opposites highlight value.
The commentator uses a series of analogies to make the point.
2
Identify the key principle
The core idea is that perfection and deficiency are known only by comparison. Without deficiency, perfection would not be appreciated.
This is a form of the 'principle of opposites'.
3
Reflect on divine justice
The passage concludes that all such disparities are justice, not injustice. Consider how this applies to reward and punishment in the Hereafter.
The ransom of animals for humans is used as an analogy for divine justice.

من بحر التوحيد فيه فيه غرق طوائف من القاصرين، ولم يعلموا أن ذلك غامض لا يعقله إلا العالمون، ووراء هذا البحر سرُّ القدر الذي تحيّر فيه الأكثرون، ومنع من إفشاء سرّه المكاشفون)، ورحم الله سيدي محمد وفا من المعارف بما أكرم به عباده الصالحين . (١) قوله : (لكن ... إلى آخره ظهر من كلام المحشي أن دليل العدم هو نفس الصنع المذكور في قوله : «تجد به صنعاً»، فكأن المصنف قال: «لكن به قام صنع دال على جواز العدم، وهذا معلوم من قوله : «تجد به صنعاً فلا فائدة فيه، إلا أن يقال : محطّ الفائدة في وصف الصنع بالدلالة على جواز العدم؛ فمعنى قوله : «تجد به صنعاً ... إلى قوله : دليل العدم إلى قوله : دليل العدم . . . » أنك إذا نظرت إليه علمت به صنعة متقنة إتقاناً لم يسبق له مثال دالة على جواز عدمه؛ ف (تجد) بمعنى (تعلم)، منصب على ما قبل الاستدراك وما بعده، هذا ما ظهر من كلامه بعد التأمل). انتهى أجهوري.
Tuhfat al-Murid 'ala Jawharat al-Tawhid. His statement: 'But ... etc.' is an objection to what is suggested by his statement: 'Wondrous of wisdom in that He is the Best' – so he did not receive meditation upon His statement Exalted: 'Who perfected everything He created and began the creation of man from clay' [Sajda 32:7]; rather this poor one stopped at the clay, and did not follow the journey in the perfect man composed from it, who is never free from perfection in every temporal part that is individual and indivisible. So the Imam's word is not only concerning the existence that you witness, but rather considering existents from when the Originator originated them until the eternity of eternities. Therefore the Imam said, continuing: 'Rather every poverty and harm in this world is a deficiency in the world and an increase in the Hereafter; and every deficiency in the Hereafter in relation to a person is bliss in relation to another; for were it not for night, the value of day would not be known; were it not for illness, the healthy would not enjoy health; were it not for the Fire, the people of Paradise would not know the value of grace. And just as the ransom of the souls of humans with the souls of animals and authorizing them to slaughter them is not injustice, but rather preferring the perfect over the deficient is the essence of justice, likewise the magnification of blessings upon the inhabitants of Paradise by magnifying punishment upon the people of Hellfire, and the ransom of the people of faith with the people of disbelief is the essence of justice. And if the deficient had not been created, the perfect would not be known; and were it not for the creation of animals, the honor of humans would not appear; for perfection and deficiency appear by comparison. So the requirement of generosity and wisdom is the creation of both the perfect and the deficient. And just as cutting off a hand when it has become gangrenous to preserve the soul is justice, because it is a ransom of a perfect thing by a deficient one, similarly is the matter in the disparity among creatures in distribution in this world and the Hereafter. So all of that is justice with no injustice in it, and truth with no play in it. And this now is another ocean, great in depth, wide in breadth, turbulent in waves, near in scope to the ocean of tawhid, in which many of those of limited capacity drown; and they did not know that it is obscure, understood only by the knowledgeable. And behind this ocean is the secret of divine decree (al-qadar), in which most are perplexed, and the unveilers are prevented from disclosing its secret.' And may God have mercy on my master Muhammad Wafa when he says: I heard God in my innermost saying: I am in the kingdom alone, I do not cease; and where everything is from Me, there is no ugly; and the ugliness of ugliness is beautiful from Me. So the eye of the needle is the secret of its beauty and the spirit of its craftsmanship, so one does not find fault with it. And God Exalted has created for Paradise creatures who have never seen evil at all: houris and youths, but He made them servants for the righteous among the people of obligation; and ugliness of disbelief is only in terms of its appearance by the hands of others, and in terms of its attribution to Him Exalted, it is beautiful and lovely. We ask Him Exalted not to afflict us with exiting this world until He honors us with knowledge with which He honored His righteous servants. (1) His statement: 'But ... etc.' It appeared from the words of the glossator that the proof of non-existence is exactly the craftsmanship mentioned in His statement: 'You find in it craftsmanship', so as if the author said: 'But through it arose craftsmanship indicating the permissibility of non-existence', and this is known from His statement: 'You find in it craftsmanship', so there is no benefit in it, except that it is said: the locus of benefit is in describing the craftsmanship as indicating the permissibility of non-existence; so the meaning of His statement: 'You find in it craftsmanship ... until his statement: proof of non-existence ... until his statement: proof of non-existence ...' is that when you look at it, you come to know through it a mastered craftsmanship, mastery of which no example has preceded, indicating the permissibility of its non-existence; so 'you find' (tajid) means 'you know' (ta'lam), placed on what is before the objection and after it. This is what appeared from his words after reflection. End of al-Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'But ... etc.' from the poem Jawharat al-Tawhid. It begins by criticizing those who misunderstood Ghazali's statement ('Wondrous of wisdom...'), accusing them of stopping at the literal meaning of 'clay' without appreciating the perfection of the complete human. The commentary then expands into a theodicy: all poverty and harm in this world are actually increases in the Hereafter; deficiencies are relative and serve to highlight perfection. The reasoning is based on comparison: without opposites, values would not be known. The commentator compares justice in this world (e.g., sacrificing animals for humans) to divine justice in rewarding and punishing. The conclusion is that everything is just and true. The passage then describes this discussion as a deep ocean, beyond which lies the secret of divine decree (sirr al-qadar), which is known only to the knowledgeable. A poem by Sidi Muhammad Wafa follows, affirming that all is from God and ugliness is relative; from God, it is beautiful. Finally, a note from al-Ajhuri clarifies that the proof of non-existence (contingency) is the craftsmanship itself: one sees a perfectly crafted thing and realizes it could not have existed—it points to its own contingency. Thus the phrase 'you find in it craftsmanship' indicates the possibility of its non-existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of evil and imperfection in the world contradicts God's wisdom and justice.
Correction: Evil and imperfection are relative; they serve to highlight greater goods and are part of a perfect divine plan that includes both the perfect and the deficient.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the existence of poverty and harm in this world not unjust?
A) Because they are punishments for sin.
B) Because they are actually increases in reward in the Hereafter.
C) Because God does not care about human suffering.
D) Because they are illusions.

Key Takeaway
All apparent evils are relative and serve to highlight greater goods; this is perfect justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful loss or a lingering hardship, do you truly believe it is a relative harm that will increase your good in the Hereafter? Or are you secretly unsettled by the thought that God might be unjust? Will you commit to whispering to yourself the truth of this lesson the next time your heart trembles at a trial?
?
Think of one specific affliction you have suffered—an illness, a financial setback, or a broken relationship. Can you see how it has sharpened your perception of the blessings you often take for granted? If you cannot see it yet, will you earnestly ask God to open your heart to the wisdom concealed in that pain?
?
How does this teaching change the way you look at people who are enduring severe poverty or chronic illness? Will you resist the urge to pity them in a way that implies God was unjust, and instead remind them—and yourself—that their hardship is part of a perfect divine economy that will yield unimaginable good? Will you act on this conviction, starting today?

The Proof of Contingency: Craftsmanship as Evidence of Possible Non-Existence
Al-Ajhuri clarifies that the proof of contingency is the craftsmanship itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intricate craftsmanship of created things points to their contingency, meaning they could have not existed, and thus require a necessary creator.

Core Idea
A perfectly crafted thing shows it could have been otherwise; its existence is not necessary.

In this final gloss, al-Ajhuri clarifies the meaning of 'proof of non-existence' (دليل العدم) in the poem. The proof is simply the observed craftsmanship itself: when you see a perfectly made object, you realize it could have not existed, because its design is not necessary. This argument is central to the kalam proof for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss
Read the Arabic and translation of al-Ajhuri's note. Note how he defines 'proof of non-existence'.
The gloss is a technical clarification of a key term in the poem.
2
Identify the claim
Al-Ajhuri states that the proof of non-existence is exactly the craftsmanship itself. This means the act of creating something with perfect design is what indicates that it could have been non-existent.
This connects to the broader kalam argument: contingent things need a cause.
3
Understand the reasoning
When you look at a crafted object, you see that it is not necessary in itself—it could have been different or not exist at all. The craftsmanship is evidence of its contingency.
The phrase 'you find in it craftsmanship' (تجد به صنعاً) means you recognize its contingency.
4
Reflect on the implication
If every created thing is contingent, then the universe as a whole is contingent and requires a necessary being (God) to bring it into existence.
This is a classic kalam cosmological argument.

شرا قط ؛ من حور ،وولدان، ولكن جعلهم خدماً للصالحين من أهل التكليف، وإنما قبح الكفر من حيث ظهوره على يد الأغيار ومن حيث نسبته له تعالى فهو حسن جميل، نسأله تعالى ألا يفجعنا بالخروج من الدنيا حتى يكرمنا من المعارف بما أكرم به عباده الصالحين . (١) قوله : (لكن ... إلى آخره ظهر من كلام المحشي أن دليل العدم هو نفس الصنع المذكور في قوله : «تجد به صنعاً»، فكأن المصنف قال: «لكن به قام صنع دال على جواز العدم، وهذا معلوم من قوله : «تجد به صنعاً فلا فائدة فيه، إلا أن يقال : محطّ الفائدة في وصف الصنع بالدلالة على جواز العدم؛ فمعنى قوله : «تجد به صنعاً ... إلى قوله : دليل العدم إلى قوله : دليل العدم . . . » أنك إذا نظرت إليه علمت به صنعة متقنة إتقاناً لم يسبق له مثال دالة على جواز عدمه؛ ف (تجد) بمعنى (تعلم)، منصب على ما قبل الاستدراك وما بعده، هذا ما ظهر من كلامه بعد التأمل). انتهى أجهوري.
Tuhfat al-Murid 'ala Jawharat al-Tawhid. His statement: 'But ... etc.' is an objection to what is suggested by his statement: 'Wondrous of wisdom in that He is the Best' – so he did not receive meditation upon His statement Exalted: 'Who perfected everything He created and began the creation of man from clay' [Sajda 32:7]; rather this poor one stopped at the clay, and did not follow the journey in the perfect man composed from it, who is never free from perfection in every temporal part that is individual and indivisible. So the Imam's word is not only concerning the existence that you witness, but rather considering existents from when the Originator originated them until the eternity of eternities. Therefore the Imam said, continuing: 'Rather every poverty and harm in this world is a deficiency in the world and an increase in the Hereafter; and every deficiency in the Hereafter in relation to a person is bliss in relation to another; for were it not for night, the value of day would not be known; were it not for illness, the healthy would not enjoy health; were it not for the Fire, the people of Paradise would not know the value of grace. And just as the ransom of the souls of humans with the souls of animals and authorizing them to slaughter them is not injustice, but rather preferring the perfect over the deficient is the essence of justice, likewise the magnification of blessings upon the inhabitants of Paradise by magnifying punishment upon the people of Hellfire, and the ransom of the people of faith with the people of disbelief is the essence of justice. And if the deficient had not been created, the perfect would not be known; and were it not for the creation of animals, the honor of humans would not appear; for perfection and deficiency appear by comparison. So the requirement of generosity and wisdom is the creation of both the perfect and the deficient. And just as cutting off a hand when it has become gangrenous to preserve the soul is justice, because it is a ransom of a perfect thing by a deficient one, similarly is the matter in the disparity among creatures in distribution in this world and the Hereafter. So all of that is justice with no injustice in it, and truth with no play in it. And this now is another ocean, great in depth, wide in breadth, turbulent in waves, near in scope to the ocean of tawhid, in which many of those of limited capacity drown; and they did not know that it is obscure, understood only by the knowledgeable. And behind this ocean is the secret of divine decree (al-qadar), in which most are perplexed, and the unveilers are prevented from disclosing its secret.' And may God have mercy on my master Muhammad Wafa when he says: I heard God in my innermost saying: I am in the kingdom alone, I do not cease; and where everything is from Me, there is no ugly; and the ugliness of ugliness is beautiful from Me. So the eye of the needle is the secret of its beauty and the spirit of its craftsmanship, so one does not find fault with it. And God Exalted has created for Paradise creatures who have never seen evil at all: houris and youths, but He made them servants for the righteous among the people of obligation; and ugliness of disbelief is only in terms of its appearance by the hands of others, and in terms of its attribution to Him Exalted, it is beautiful and lovely. We ask Him Exalted not to afflict us with exiting this world until He honors us with knowledge with which He honored His righteous servants. (1) His statement: 'But ... etc.' It appeared from the words of the glossator that the proof of non-existence is exactly the craftsmanship mentioned in His statement: 'You find in it craftsmanship', so as if the author said: 'But through it arose craftsmanship indicating the permissibility of non-existence', and this is known from His statement: 'You find in it craftsmanship', so there is no benefit in it, except that it is said: the locus of benefit is in describing the craftsmanship as indicating the permissibility of non-existence; so the meaning of His statement: 'You find in it craftsmanship ... until his statement: proof of non-existence ... until his statement: proof of non-existence ...' is that when you look at it, you come to know through it a mastered craftsmanship, mastery of which no example has preceded, indicating the permissibility of its non-existence; so 'you find' (tajid) means 'you know' (ta'lam), placed on what is before the objection and after it. This is what appeared from his words after reflection. End of al-Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'But ... etc.' from the poem Jawharat al-Tawhid. It begins by criticizing those who misunderstood Ghazali's statement ('Wondrous of wisdom...'), accusing them of stopping at the literal meaning of 'clay' without appreciating the perfection of the complete human. The commentary then expands into a theodicy: all poverty and harm in this world are actually increases in the Hereafter; deficiencies are relative and serve to highlight perfection. The reasoning is based on comparison: without opposites, values would not be known. The commentator compares justice in this world (e.g., sacrificing animals for humans) to divine justice in rewarding and punishing. The conclusion is that everything is just and true. The passage then describes this discussion as a deep ocean, beyond which lies the secret of divine decree (sirr al-qadar), which is known only to the knowledgeable. A poem by Sidi Muhammad Wafa follows, affirming that all is from God and ugliness is relative; from God, it is beautiful. Finally, a note from al-Ajhuri clarifies that the proof of non-existence (contingency) is the craftsmanship itself: one sees a perfectly crafted thing and realizes it could not have existed—it points to its own contingency. Thus the phrase 'you find in it craftsmanship' indicates the possibility of its non-existence.

Key Terms
دليل العدم
صنع
جواز العدم
تجد
تعلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of contingency is something external to the object, like a separate argument.
Correction: Al-Ajhuri clarifies that the proof is the craftsmanship itself; the object's design directly indicates its possible non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ajhuri, what is the 'proof of non-existence' (دليل العدم)?
A) An independent argument from design.
B) The craftsmanship (الصنع) itself.
C) The fact that things change.
D) The name of the author.

Key Takeaway
The intricate design of creation itself is the proof that it could have been otherwise, establishing contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body—its complexity and fragility—do you see the craftsmanship that proves you could have never existed? How would your gratitude change if you truly felt this contingency every moment?
?
Think of one blessing you have that you consider 'normal' or 'deserved'. If you knew with certainty that it could have not been, would you treat it the same way? What would you do differently starting today?
?
The proof of contingency is not a distant theory—it is in the very objects you use daily. Choose one object right now and examine it as a sign. What does it tell you about the One who brought it into existence? How does that move you to respond?

The Relativity of Good and Evil in Divine Justice
Refutation of the objection that God must create only the best, using Quran and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how apparent deficiencies and inequalities in creation are actually manifestations of divine justice and wisdom, as they serve to highlight perfection and provide contrast.

Core Idea
Evil and deficiency are relative; without them, good could not be appreciated or known.

The commentator responds to an objection that God must create only the best by showing that apparent imperfections are not absolute but relative. Through examples like night and day, illness and health, and the difference between humans and animals, he demonstrates that deficiency in one context is increase in another, and that preferring the perfect over the imperfect is the very essence of justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the first part where the commentator quotes the objector and then refutes by referencing the Quranic verse about creation from clay and the perfect human.
2
Examine the examples of relativity
Notice how the Imam uses examples like night and day, illness and health, and Fire and Paradise to show that deficiency in one place is increase in another.
These examples illustrate the principle of contrast.
3
Understand the principle of justice
The passage explains that preferring the perfect over the imperfect is justice, as seen in animals for humans, believers for disbelievers, and amputation for the body.
This principle applies to all disparities in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لَكِنْ ... إلخ ) (۱) : استدرالاً على ما يشعر به قوله : (بديع الحكم من أنه الأحسن، فهذا لم يحظ بتأمل في قوله سبحانه: ﴿ الَّذِي أَحْسَنَ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقَةٌ وَبَدَأَ خَلَقَ الْإِنسَينِ مِن طِينٍ) [السجدة: ٧] ، بل وقف هذا المسكين عند الطين، ولم يتابع السير في الإنسان الكامل المكون منه، والذي لا ينفك عن الكمال في كل جزء زماني فرد لا يقبل التجزؤ، فكلمة الإمام ليست في حق الوجود الذي تُشاهده فحسب، بل باعتبار الموجودات مذ فطرها الفاطر وإلى أبد الآباد، ولذا قال الإمام متابعاً: (بل كلُّ فقر وضر في الدنيا فهو نقصان من الدنيا وزيادة في الآخرة، وكلُّ نقص في الآخرة بالإضافة إلى شخص فهو نعيم بالإضافة إلى غيره ؛ إذ لولا الليل لما عرف قدر النهار، ولولا المرضُ لما تنعم الأصحاء بالصحة ، ولولا النار لما عرف أهل الجنة قدرَ النعمة . وكما أن فداء أرواح الإنس بأرواح البهائم وتسليطهم على ذبحها ليس بظلم، بل تقديم الكامل على الناقص عين العدل، فكذلك تفخيم النعم على سكان الجنان بتعظيم العقوبة على أهل النيران، وفداء أهل الإيمان بأهل الكفران عين العدل، وما لم يخلق الناقص ، لا يُعرف الكامل، ولولا خلقُ البهائم، لما ظهر شرفُ الإنس؛ فإن الكمال والنقص يظهر بالإضافة، فمقتضى الجود والحكمة خلق الكامل والناقص جميعاً، وكما أن قطع اليد إذا تأكلتُ إبقاء على الروح عدل؛ لأنه فداء كامل بناقص، فكذلك الأمر في التفاوت الذي بين الخلق في القسمة في الدنيا والآخرة، فكل ذلك عدل لا جورَ فيه وحقٌّ لا لعب فيه، وهذا الآن بحرٌ آخرُ عظيم العمق، واسع الأطراف، مضطرب الأمواج، قريب في السعة من بحر التوحيد فيه فيه غرق طوائف من القاصرين، ولم يعلموا أن ذلك غامض لا يعقله إلا العالمون، ووراء هذا البحر سرُّ القدر الذي تحيّر فيه الأكثرون، ومنع من إفشاء سرّه المكاشفون)، ورحم الله سيدي محمد وفا إذ يقول : سمعت الله في سري يقولُ : أنا في الملك وحدي لا أزولُ وحيث الكل عني لا قبيح وقبح القبح من حيثي جميل فخرم الإبرة سر جمالها ، وروح إبداعها، فلا تعاب به، وقد خلق الله تعالى للجنة خلقاً لم يروا شرا قط ؛ من حور ،وولدان، ولكن جعلهم خدماً للصالحين من أهل التكليف، وإنما قبح الكفر من حيث ظهوره على يد الأغيار ومن حيث نسبته له تعالى فهو حسن جميل، نسأله تعالى ألا يفجعنا بالخروج من الدنيا حتى يكرمنا من المعارف بما أكرم به عباده الصالحين.
His statement: (but ... etc.): An objection to what is suggested by his statement: (the ingenious Maker of wisdom that it is the best). This person did not contemplate His saying, may He be exalted: 'Who made everything He created good and began the creation of man from clay' [Sajda 32:7], but rather this wretched one stopped at the clay and did not follow the journey into the perfect human being composed from it, who is never free from perfection in every individual temporal part that does not admit division. So the Imam's word is not only about the existence you witness, but with regard to existents from when the Creator created them until eternity everlasting. Hence the Imam said, following up: 'Rather every poverty and harm in this world is a deficiency in this world and an increase in the Hereafter. And every deficiency in the Hereafter relative to a person is bliss relative to another. For were it not for the night, the value of the day would not be known; were it not for illness, the healthy would not enjoy health; were it not for the Fire, the people of Paradise would not know the extent of blessing. And just as ransoming human souls with the souls of animals and empowering them to slaughter them is not injustice but rather preferring the perfect over the imperfect is the very essence of justice, so too magnifying blessings upon the inhabitants of Paradise by magnifying punishment upon the people of the Fire, and ransoming the people of faith with the people of disbelief is the very essence of justice. Without creating the imperfect, the perfect would not be known; were it not for the creation of animals, the honor of humans would not appear. For perfection and imperfection appear by relation. So the requirement of generosity and wisdom is to create both the perfect and the imperfect. And just as cutting off a gangrenous hand to preserve the soul is just because it is a ransom of the perfect for the imperfect, so too is the matter regarding the disparity among creatures in distribution in this world and the Hereafter. All of that is justice with no injustice, truth with no play. This now is another ocean, immense in depth, wide in expanse, agitated in waves, near in breadth to the ocean of Oneness, in which groups of the deficient have drowned, and they do not know that it is obscure, understood only by the knowers. Beyond this ocean is the secret of predestination, about which most have been perplexed, and the unveilers have been forbidden from disclosing it. May God have mercy on my master Muhammad Wafa when he says: I heard God in my secret saying: I am in the kingdom alone, I do not cease; and everything from Me has no ugly; the ugliness of ugliness from My perspective is beautiful. So the hole of the needle is the secret of its beauty and the spirit of its artistry; so it is not faulted for it. And God Almighty created for Paradise creatures who have never seen evil at all: houris and boys, but He made them servants for the righteous among the people of obligation. And indeed the ugliness of disbelief is only from its appearing at the hands of others, and from its attribution to Him Almighty it is beautiful and good. We ask Him Almighty not to afflict us by leaving this world until He honors us with knowledge as He honored His righteous servants.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and defense of divine wisdom in creation, responding to an objection that the world should be perfectly good without any apparent evil or deficiency. The commentator begins by quoting the objector's claim that the ingenious Maker (بديع الحكم) must create only the best (الأحسن). He then refutes this by pointing to the Quranic verse 'Who made everything He created good' (السجدة: 7), noting that the objector stopped at the creation of man from clay without considering the perfect human being that comes from it. The commentator then quotes an Imam who explains that all apparent deficiencies in this world are relative: they are increases in the Hereafter. Deficiencies in the Hereafter relative to one person are blessings relative to another. He gives examples: night/day, illness/health, and the Fire/Paradise are contrasts that allow appreciation of good. He extends the principle to show that differences in status among creatures (humans vs. animals, believers vs. disbelievers, inhabitants of Paradise vs. Fire) are all just because they involve preference of the perfect over the imperfect. The commentator then declares this topic as a vast ocean of knowledge, beyond which lies the secret of predestination (سر القدر). He supports his argument with a mystical poem by Muhammad Wafa, stating that all things from God are good, and ugliness is only relative to the perception of others. Finally, he mentions that God created some beings (like houris and boys) who never see evil, yet they serve the righteous, further illustrating that apparent imperfection serves a purpose. The passage ends with a prayer for true knowledge.

Key Terms
استدراك
بديع الحكم
الأحسن
الكمال والنقص
العدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world should be perfectly good without any imperfection or deficiency.
Correction: Apparent deficiencies are relative and necessary for the recognition of perfection; they are just in God's wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why are apparent deficiencies in this world not unjust?
A) Because God is free to do as He wills.
B) Because they are always compensated in the Hereafter, and they allow for the recognition of perfection through contrast.
C) Because the world is inherently imperfect and God cannot change that.
D) Because deficiency only appears in this world and not in the Hereafter.

Key Takeaway
Apparent imperfections in creation are not unjust but are relative contrasts that highlight perfection and are just in God's wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial—illness, poverty, or loss—do you see it only as a deficiency, or do you truly believe it may be an increase in the Hereafter? What would change in your emotional response if you lived this truth tonight?
?
The author says without the Fire, the people of Paradise would not fully know God's blessing. Do you ever thank God for saving you from eternal loss, or do you take His mercy for granted? How often do you let the reality of Hell deepen your gratitude?
?
If you knew your suffering was the means for another's perfection—like the animals slaughtered for human benefit—would you willingly accept your role? Are you willing to be the 'imperfect' so that God's wisdom and justice may be displayed?

The Ocean of Divine Wisdom and the Secret of Predestination
Warning about the depth of the topic and mystical affirmation that all from God is beautiful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the topic of divine wisdom is vast and that many fail to grasp it, and that everything from God, including what appears ugly, is beautiful in its own right.

Core Idea
The secret of predestination is a deep ocean that only the knowers can comprehend; all things from God are beautiful.

The commentator warns that the discussion of predestination is a vast ocean in which many have drowned. He then quotes a mystical poem stating that everything from God is beautiful, and ugliness is only relative. He concludes by giving the example of houris and boys who never see evil but serve the righteous, and states that disbelief is ugly only when attributed to others, but from God it is beautiful.

Guided Path
1
Read the ocean metaphor
Read the part where the commentator calls this topic a vast ocean and warns that many have drowned in it.
2
Study the mystical poem
Read the poem by Muhammad Wafa which states that everything from God is beautiful, and ugliness is relative.
The poem uses the example of the needle's hole.
3
Analyze the example of houris and boys
Note that God created beings who never see evil, yet they serve the righteous, showing that apparent evil is not intrinsic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لَكِنْ ... إلخ ) (۱) : استدرالاً على ما يشعر به قوله : (بديع الحكم من أنه الأحسن، فهذا لم يحظ بتأمل في قوله سبحانه: ﴿ الَّذِي أَحْسَنَ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقَةٌ وَبَدَأَ خَلَقَ الْإِنسَينِ مِن طِينٍ) [السجدة: ٧] ، بل وقف هذا المسكين عند الطين، ولم يتابع السير في الإنسان الكامل المكون منه، والذي لا ينفك عن الكمال في كل جزء زماني فرد لا يقبل التجزؤ، فكلمة الإمام ليست في حق الوجود الذي تُشاهده فحسب، بل باعتبار الموجودات مذ فطرها الفاطر وإلى أبد الآباد، ولذا قال الإمام متابعاً: (بل كلُّ فقر وضر في الدنيا فهو نقصان من الدنيا وزيادة في الآخرة، وكلُّ نقص في الآخرة بالإضافة إلى شخص فهو نعيم بالإضافة إلى غيره ؛ إذ لولا الليل لما عرف قدر النهار، ولولا المرضُ لما تنعم الأصحاء بالصحة ، ولولا النار لما عرف أهل الجنة قدرَ النعمة . وكما أن فداء أرواح الإنس بأرواح البهائم وتسليطهم على ذبحها ليس بظلم، بل تقديم الكامل على الناقص عين العدل، فكذلك تفخيم النعم على سكان الجنان بتعظيم العقوبة على أهل النيران، وفداء أهل الإيمان بأهل الكفران عين العدل، وما لم يخلق الناقص ، لا يُعرف الكامل، ولولا خلقُ البهائم، لما ظهر شرفُ الإنس؛ فإن الكمال والنقص يظهر بالإضافة، فمقتضى الجود والحكمة خلق الكامل والناقص جميعاً، وكما أن قطع اليد إذا تأكلتُ إبقاء على الروح عدل؛ لأنه فداء كامل بناقص، فكذلك الأمر في التفاوت الذي بين الخلق في القسمة في الدنيا والآخرة، فكل ذلك عدل لا جورَ فيه وحقٌّ لا لعب فيه، وهذا الآن بحرٌ آخرُ عظيم العمق، واسع الأطراف، مضطرب الأمواج، قريب في السعة من بحر التوحيد فيه فيه غرق طوائف من القاصرين، ولم يعلموا أن ذلك غامض لا يعقله إلا العالمون، ووراء هذا البحر سرُّ القدر الذي تحيّر فيه الأكثرون، ومنع من إفشاء سرّه المكاشفون)، ورحم الله سيدي محمد وفا إذ يقول : سمعت الله في سري يقولُ : أنا في الملك وحدي لا أزولُ وحيث الكل عني لا قبيح وقبح القبح من حيثي جميل فخرم الإبرة سر جمالها ، وروح إبداعها، فلا تعاب به، وقد خلق الله تعالى للجنة خلقاً لم يروا شرا قط ؛ من حور ،وولدان، ولكن جعلهم خدماً للصالحين من أهل التكليف، وإنما قبح الكفر من حيث ظهوره على يد الأغيار ومن حيث نسبته له تعالى فهو حسن جميل، نسأله تعالى ألا يفجعنا بالخروج من الدنيا حتى يكرمنا من المعارف بما أكرم به عباده الصالحين.
His statement: (but ... etc.): An objection to what is suggested by his statement: (the ingenious Maker of wisdom that it is the best). This person did not contemplate His saying, may He be exalted: 'Who made everything He created good and began the creation of man from clay' [Sajda 32:7], but rather this wretched one stopped at the clay and did not follow the journey into the perfect human being composed from it, who is never free from perfection in every individual temporal part that does not admit division. So the Imam's word is not only about the existence you witness, but with regard to existents from when the Creator created them until eternity everlasting. Hence the Imam said, following up: 'Rather every poverty and harm in this world is a deficiency in this world and an increase in the Hereafter. And every deficiency in the Hereafter relative to a person is bliss relative to another. For were it not for the night, the value of the day would not be known; were it not for illness, the healthy would not enjoy health; were it not for the Fire, the people of Paradise would not know the extent of blessing. And just as ransoming human souls with the souls of animals and empowering them to slaughter them is not injustice but rather preferring the perfect over the imperfect is the very essence of justice, so too magnifying blessings upon the inhabitants of Paradise by magnifying punishment upon the people of the Fire, and ransoming the people of faith with the people of disbelief is the very essence of justice. Without creating the imperfect, the perfect would not be known; were it not for the creation of animals, the honor of humans would not appear. For perfection and imperfection appear by relation. So the requirement of generosity and wisdom is to create both the perfect and the imperfect. And just as cutting off a gangrenous hand to preserve the soul is just because it is a ransom of the perfect for the imperfect, so too is the matter regarding the disparity among creatures in distribution in this world and the Hereafter. All of that is justice with no injustice, truth with no play. This now is another ocean, immense in depth, wide in expanse, agitated in waves, near in breadth to the ocean of Oneness, in which groups of the deficient have drowned, and they do not know that it is obscure, understood only by the knowers. Beyond this ocean is the secret of predestination, about which most have been perplexed, and the unveilers have been forbidden from disclosing it. May God have mercy on my master Muhammad Wafa when he says: I heard God in my secret saying: I am in the kingdom alone, I do not cease; and everything from Me has no ugly; the ugliness of ugliness from My perspective is beautiful. So the hole of the needle is the secret of its beauty and the spirit of its artistry; so it is not faulted for it. And God Almighty created for Paradise creatures who have never seen evil at all: houris and boys, but He made them servants for the righteous among the people of obligation. And indeed the ugliness of disbelief is only from its appearing at the hands of others, and from its attribution to Him Almighty it is beautiful and good. We ask Him Almighty not to afflict us by leaving this world until He honors us with knowledge as He honored His righteous servants.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and defense of divine wisdom in creation, responding to an objection that the world should be perfectly good without any apparent evil or deficiency. The commentator begins by quoting the objector's claim that the ingenious Maker (بديع الحكم) must create only the best (الأحسن). He then refutes this by pointing to the Quranic verse 'Who made everything He created good' (السجدة: 7), noting that the objector stopped at the creation of man from clay without considering the perfect human being that comes from it. The commentator then quotes an Imam who explains that all apparent deficiencies in this world are relative: they are increases in the Hereafter. Deficiencies in the Hereafter relative to one person are blessings relative to another. He gives examples: night/day, illness/health, and the Fire/Paradise are contrasts that allow appreciation of good. He extends the principle to show that differences in status among creatures (humans vs. animals, believers vs. disbelievers, inhabitants of Paradise vs. Fire) are all just because they involve preference of the perfect over the imperfect. The commentator then declares this topic as a vast ocean of knowledge, beyond which lies the secret of predestination (سر القدر). He supports his argument with a mystical poem by Muhammad Wafa, stating that all things from God are good, and ugliness is only relative to the perception of others. Finally, he mentions that God created some beings (like houris and boys) who never see evil, yet they serve the righteous, further illustrating that apparent imperfection serves a purpose. The passage ends with a prayer for true knowledge.

Key Terms
سر القدر
المكاشفون
القبيح
الحسن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some things in creation are inherently ugly or evil.
Correction: Everything from God is beautiful; ugliness is only relative to perception and not absolute.

Checkpoint
According to the poem quoted in the passage, how does God view what appears ugly?
A) As ugly
B) As beautiful
C) As neutral
D) As evil

Key Takeaway
The secret of predestination is a deep ocean; from God's perspective, all things are beautiful and serve a divine purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something you consider evil or unjust, do you truly believe it is beautiful from God's perspective? How does that belief confront your immediate emotional reaction? Will you sit with that discomfort until you see the beauty?
?
The secret of predestination has perplexed most people, and the unveilers are forbidden to disclose it. Have you ever tried to fully understand why God allows certain things? Are you willing to accept that you may never fully grasp it, yet still trust that all from Him is beautiful?
?
The poet hears God say, 'everything from Me has no ugly.' Look at your own life: is there something—a hardship, a sin, a loss—that you still see as ugly? Will you ask God tonight to show you the beauty in it, even if it hurts?

The Gradual Reformative Power of Prayer
Relates a hadith about a sinful youth whose prayer eventually reformed him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how even imperfect prayer can gradually reform a person's character, as illustrated by the hadith of the Ansari youth.

Core Idea
Prayer's reformative power is gradual, not immediate.

This lesson presents a powerful story from the Prophet's time about a young man who prayed regularly but continued committing sins. The Prophet's insight that his prayer would eventually prevent him from evil teaches us an important lesson about the cumulative effect of worship. Let's explore this hadith and its implications for our own spiritual journey.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage about the youth from the Ansar. Notice how the Prophet described his situation and his prophecy.
The hadith shows the Prophet's knowledge of the unseen.
2
Understand the gradual effect
Reflect on the phrase 'سنهاه يوماً ما' – 'will prevent him one day'. This indicates that prayer's impact may take time.
3
Connect to your own practice
Consider how this hadith encourages persistence in prayer even when we struggle with sins.
This is a common experience for many believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد رُوي أن فتى من الأنصار كان يصلي الصلوات مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ولا يدعُ شيئاً من الفواحش إلا ارتكبه، فوصف الرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فقال: «إنَّ صلاته ستنهاه يوماً ما»، فلم يلبث أن تاب وحسُنَتْ توبتُه ، فقال : ألم أقل لكم: إنَّ صلاته ستنهاه يوماً ما ؟! وقال بعض المفسرين : (الصلاة) عرس الموحدين ؛ فإنه يجتمع فيها ألوان العبادة؛ كما أن العرس يجتمعُ فيه ألوان الطعام، فإذا صلى العبد ركعتين يقول الله تعالى: عبدي ؛ مع ضعفِكَ أتيت بألوان العبادة؛ قياماً وركوعاً وسجوداً وقراءة وتهليلاً وتحميداً وتكبيراً وسلاماً فأنا مع جلالتي وعظمتى لا يجملُ منّى أن أمنعَكَ جَنَّةٌ فيها ألوان النعيم، أوجبتُ لك الجنَّةَ بنعيمها كما عبدتني بألوان العبادة، وأكرمك برؤيتي كما عرفتني بالوحدانية، فإني لطيف أقبلُ عذرك، وأقبلُ الخير منك برحمتي، فإني أجد مَنْ أعذِّبُهُ من الكفَّار بالنار، وأنت لا تجد إلهاً غيري يغفرُ سيئاتِكَ، عندي لك بكلِّ ركعة قصر في الجنة وحوراء، وبكلِّ سجدة نظرة إلى وجهي .
And it has been narrated that a youth from the Ansar used to pray the prayers with the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and he would not leave any immoral act except he committed it. So the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, described him and said: 'Indeed, his prayer will prevent him one day.' He did not delay until he repented and his repentance became good, so he said: 'Did I not tell you: indeed, his prayer will prevent him one day?' And some of the exegetes said: Prayer is the wedding of the monotheists; for in it are gathered all types of worship, just as in a wedding all types of food are gathered. So when the servant prays two rak‘ahs, Allah says: My servant, despite your weakness you have come with types of worship – standing, bowing, prostrating, recitation, glorification, praise, takbir, and salams. So I, with My majesty and greatness, it is not befitting of Me to prevent you from a garden in which are types of bliss. I have obligated for you the Garden with its bliss just as you worshipped Me with types of worship, and I honor you with vision of Me just as you knew Me with oneness. For I am gentle, I accept your excuse, and I accept good from you by My mercy. For I find those whom I punish among the disbelievers with fire, while you find no god other than Me to forgive your sins. With Me, for every rak‘ah there is a palace in Paradise and a houri, and for every prostration a glance at My face.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage consists of two parts that illustrate the virtue and effect of prayer. First, it narrates a hadith about a young man from the Ansar who prayed regularly with the Prophet but continued to commit grave sins. The Prophet prophesied that his prayer would eventually prevent him from evil, and indeed the youth later repented, confirming the Prophet's words. This story demonstrates that even if a person prays while persisting in sin, the prayer itself has a reforming power over time. Second, the author quotes some exegetes (mufassirin) who describe prayer as the 'wedding of the monotheists' because it gathers various acts of worship just as a wedding gathers various foods. They then present a divine address: when the servant prays two rak‘ahs with all those components, Allah promises that He will not withhold Paradise, and for every rak‘ah a palace and houri, and for every prostration a direct vision of His face. This emphasizes the immense reward of prayer, linking each physical act to a specific spiritual reward, including the ultimate vision of Allah (ru’ya).

Key Terms
الفواحش
الأنصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if you pray while sinning, your prayer is invalid or useless.
Correction: The hadith shows that prayer still has value and can gradually reform the person, even if they continue sinning for a time.

Checkpoint
What did the Prophet say about the youth who prayed but continued sinning?
A) His prayer is invalid
B) His prayer will eventually prevent him from sin
C) He should stop praying
D) He is a hypocrite

Key Takeaway
Prayer has a gradual reforming effect; even if you struggle with sins, do not abandon prayer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have struggled with a sin repeatedly despite praying regularly. Have you ever felt that your prayer is ineffective? How does this hadith challenge that feeling? What will you do differently starting today?
?
Imagine you are that youth: praying but still sinning. Do you have the patience to keep praying without seeing immediate results? Write down one specific sin you will persist in praying against, trusting Allah's timing.
?
The Prophet did not condemn the youth but gave a promise. How can you extend that same patience to others who are struggling? Name one person you can encourage today to keep praying despite their sins.

Prayer as the Wedding of Monotheists and Its Divine Reward
Elaborates on prayer's reward using the metaphor of a wedding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the description of prayer as the wedding of monotheists and the immense rewards promised for each part of the prayer.

Core Idea
Prayer gathers all forms of worship and earns rewards including Paradise and vision of Allah.

Some exegetes gave a beautiful description of prayer as the wedding of monotheists because it combines various acts of worship. In this lesson, we will explore this metaphor and the divine address that follows, detailing the reward for each element of prayer. This passage highlights the generosity of Allah towards His servants.

Read the mufassir's description

This metaphor emphasizes the gathering of diverse worship acts.

Understand the divine address

3
Reflect on the rewards

This is a powerful motivation for excellence in prayer.

The First Necessary Attributes: Existence, Pre-Eternity, and Permanence
Introduction to divine attributes and the first three necessary attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that existence is the essential attribute of Allah and that pre-eternity and permanence are also necessary attributes, along with the grammatical function of the 'fa' of eloquent exposition.

Core Idea
Allah's existence is an essential attribute, not separate from His essence.

Before exploring the specific attributes of Allah, we must understand the categories into which they are divided. This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid introduces the essential attribute of existence and two other necessary attributes: pre-eternity and permanence. The author also uses a distinctive grammatical structure to signal that he is answering a question about what is necessary for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first a heading listing categories, then a line of poetry defining three attributes, then a commentary explaining the 'fa'.
The passage includes both matn (text) and sharh (commentary).
2
Identify the categories of attributes
The heading lists four categories: essential (nafsiyya), negative (salbiyya), attributes of meaning (ma'ani), and attributes of meaning-derived states (ma'nawiyya). The first one is the focus here.
Essential attributes are those that are inseparable from the essence.
3
Focus on the essential attribute: existence
The text states: 'The essential attribute is one, which is existence.' Existence is not a separate quality added to Allah; it is what He is.
This counters the misconception that Allah's existence is an accident or added attribute.
4
Understand the three necessary attributes listed
The poem says: 'So necessary for Him are existence, pre-eternity, similarly permanence unmixed with non-existence.' These three are foundational.
Pre-eternity (qidam) means without beginning, permanence (baqa') means without end.
5
Examine the 'fa' of eloquent exposition
The commentary explains that the 'fa' at the start of the line is 'fa' al-fasiha, indicating an implied condition: 'If you want to know what is necessary for Allah, then [the answer is]...'
This grammatical point shows the author's pedagogical style.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات الواجبة لله تعالى (النفسيَّةُ والسلبية والمعاني والمعنوية):]
أ. الصفةُ النفسية واحدة وهي الوجود]
٢٣ - فَوَاجِبُ لَهُ الوُجُودُ وَالقِدَمُ كَذَا بَقَاءُ لَا يُشَابُ بِالعَدَمَ
قوله : (فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ ... إلخ)؛ أي: إذا أردت معرفة ما يجب له تعالى فأقول لك : واجب له ... إلى آخره، فالفاء فاء الفصيحة، والضمير المجرور عائد عليه تعالى .
The necessary attributes of Allah Almighty (the nafsiyya (essential), the salbiyya (negative), the ma'ani (attributes of meaning), and the ma'nawiyya (attributes of meaning-derived [states])):] a. The essential attribute is one, which is existence. 23 - So necessary for Him are existence, pre-eternity, similarly permanence unmixed with non-existence. His statement: 'So necessary for Him ... etc.' meaning: If you wish to know what is necessary for Him Almighty, I say to you: Necessary for Him ... etc. So the fa' is the fa' of eloquent exposition, and the enclosed pronoun refers back to Him Almighty.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage introduces the section on Allah's necessary attributes (al-sifāt al-wājiba). The author divides them into categories: essential (nafsiyya), negative (salbiyya), attributes of meaning (ma'ani), and attributes of meaning-derived states (ma'nawiyya). He begins with the essential attribute: existence (al-wujud). Then he quotes the verse from the poem: 'So necessary for Him are existence, pre-eternity, similarly permanence unmixed with non-existence.' The commentary explains the structure: the word 'fa' is the fa' of fasiha (eloquent exposition), indicating that when you want to know what is necessary for Allah, the answer is: necessary for Him are these attributes. The pronoun 'lahu' refers to Allah.

Key Terms
الصفات الواجبة
الصفة النفسية
الوجود
القدم
البقاء
الفاء الفصيحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that existence is a separate attribute added to Allah's essence, like an accident.
Correction: The passage identifies existence as the essential attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya), meaning it is not additional but identical to the essence itself.

Checkpoint
What is the first essential attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya) of Allah mentioned in this passage?
A) Pre-eternity.
B) Permanence.
C) Existence.
D) Oneness.

Key Takeaway
Allah's existence is an essential attribute, inseparable from His essence, and along with pre-eternity and permanence, these three form the foundation of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's existence is His very essence and not an added attribute, then what does this mean for how you conceive of Allah in your worship? Are you ever tempted to think of Allah's existence as something separate from His nature, like a quality added to a person? How does this lesson correct that? What practical change does this require in your daily awareness of Allah?
?
The attributes of pre-eternity and permanence are necessary for Allah. In your life, you are surrounded by things that begin and end. How does the reality of Allah's pre-eternity and permanence challenge your attachment to temporary things? What concrete step can you take today to prioritize the Eternal over the fleeting?
?
The phrase 'unmixed with non-existence' emphasizes that Allah's permanence is absolute — He is always present and never absent. Are there moments when you forget His constant presence? What habits can you develop to remember that Allah's permanence is a reality that should shape every moment of your life?

Essential Existence: Allah's Existence Is for Himself
Definition of essential existence and its contrast with contingent existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of essential existence (al-wujud al-dhati) and why it distinguishes Allah's existence from contingent existence.

Core Idea
Allah's existence is for His essence, not from any cause—including Himself.

The author begins by defining one of Allah's essential attributes: existence. But this is not existence as we know it. Divine existence is unique: it is not given by any cause, nor is it self-caused. In this passage, you will see how the term 'essential' captures this radical independence and why it requires a careful qualifier to avoid misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own wording come through.
2
Understand 'li-dhatihi la li-illah'
Focus on the phrase 'that His existence is for His essence not from a cause.' This is the core definition. Note that 'for His essence' means it is not dependent on any other.
3
Why is self-causation rejected?
The author adds a clarification: it does not mean that the essence caused itself. No sane person would say that. This prevents a common misunderstanding.
The qualifier (muhtazar) is necessary to exclude the mistaken idea of self-causation.
4
Contrast with contingent existence
The passage ends by contrasting essential existence with contingent existence (like ours), which is caused by Allah's action. This contrast clarifies the meaning.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Think about why this distinction matters for Islamic theology. It establishes Allah's complete independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الوُجُودُ) أي : الذاتي ; بمعنى : أن وجوده لذاته لا لعلة؛ أي: إن الغير ليس مؤثراً في وجوده ،تعالى، وليس المراد أن الذات أثرتُ في نفسها ; إذ لا يقوله عاقل ، وإِنَّما ضاق عليهم التعبير ، فثمرة القيد تظهرُ في المحترز، وأما الوجود غير الذاتي كوجودنا فهو بفعله تعالى .
His statement: (Al-Wujūd) meaning: the essential (al-dhātī); meaning: that His existence is for His essence not from a cause; that is: the other is not effective in His existence, exalted, and it is not meant that the essence effected itself; for no sane person says that, but rather expression was too limited for them, so the benefit of the restriction appears in the qualifier. As for non-essential existence like our existence, it is by His action, exalted.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of existence (al-wujūd) as an essential attribute of Allah. The author explains that 'essential existence' means that Allah's existence is for His essence (li-dhātihi) and not from any cause (lā li-'illah). This negates any external cause for His existence. He further clarifies that it does not mean the essence caused its own existence, as that would be absurd. The term 'essential' is used to distinguish from contingent existence, which is caused by Allah's action. The restriction is useful to exclude contingent existence from the attribute.

Key Terms
الوجود الذاتي
الوجود غير الذاتي
علة
محترز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence for itself means Allah caused His own existence.
Correction: The author explicitly denies self-causation, saying 'no sane person says that'. Essential existence means no cause at all, not even self.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'essential existence' (الوجود الذاتي) mean?
A) Existence that comes from a cause
B) Existence that is self-caused
C) Existence that is not caused by any other
D) Existence that is contingent

Key Takeaway
Allah's existence is essential, meaning it is not caused by anything, not even by His own essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah’s existence is not caused by anything, even Himself, then why do you sometimes act as if your own existence depends on your own efforts? How can you shift your reliance to Him alone?
?
This passage says contingent existence is by Allah’s action. How does knowing that your very existence is a constant gift from Him change the way you approach your daily tasks and struggles?
?
The author says ‘no sane person’ claims Allah caused Himself. Yet, do you ever subconsciously believe you can cause your own success or happiness? What specific steps will you take to root out that self-reliance and truly depend on Allah?

Existence and the Existent: Identity or Additional?
The author presents the debate on existence and analyzes al-Ash'ari's statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether existence (al-wujūd) is identical to the existent (al-mawjūd) and the correct interpretation of al-Ash'ari's position.

Core Idea
Existence is a conceptual matter, not an extra attribute added to the essence in external reality.

One of the foundational debates in Islamic theology concerns the relationship between existence and the thing that exists. Is existence exactly the same as the essence, or is it something additional? This passage presents the dispute and clarifies the authentic Ash'ari position through scholarly interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the debate.
Notice the two interpretations of al-Ash'ari's statement.
2
Identify the key positions
Identify the disagreement, al-Ash'ari's statement, the literal interpretation, and the verified scholars' interpretation.
The verified scholars include figures like al-Sa'd al-Taftāzānī.
3
Analyze the two interpretations
Understand why the literal reading leads to calling existence an attribute only as a concession, while the interpretive reading avoids that concession.
Focus on the concept of 'conceptual matter' (amr iʿtibārī).
4
Apply the correct understanding
Reflect on why the verified view is 'the truth from which there is no escape'.
This distinction affects how we speak of Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلف في الوجود: هل هو عين الموجود أو غيره كما سيأتي؟ فقال الأشعري : (الوجود عين الموجود، واختلف العلماء في فهم المراد من عبارة الأشعري، فبعضُهم أبقاها على ظاهرها وعليه يكون في عد الوجودِ صفةٌ تسامح ; لأنَّه يقعُ صفةٌ في مجرَّد اللفظ، كأن يقول : الله موجود . والمحققون كالسعد وأضرابه أوّلوا عبارةَ الأشعري فقالوا : ليس المراد العينية حقيقة، بل المراد أنه ليس زائداً على الذات في الخارج بحيث تصح رؤيته، فلا ينافي أنَّه أمر اعتباري، وهو الحقُّ الذي لا محيص عنه، وعليه فلا يكون في عد الوجودِ تسامح .
And there is disagreement about existence: is it identical to the existent or other than it, as will come? Al-Ashʿarī said: 'Existence is identical to the existent.' And the scholars differed in understanding the intended meaning of al-Ashʿarī's statement. Some of them left it on its apparent meaning, and accordingly counting existence as an attribute is a concession, because it occurs as an attribute merely in wording, like saying 'Allah exists'. And the verified scholars, like al-Saʿd and his peers, interpreted al-Ashʿarī's statement and said: the intended meaning is not literal identity, but rather the intended meaning is that it is not additional to the essence in external reality such that it could be seen. So it does not contradict that it is a conceptual matter, and that is the truth from which there is no escape, and accordingly there is no concession in counting existence as an attribute.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the theological debate about whether existence (al-wujūd) is identical to the existent (al-mawjūd) or something additional. The author mentions that al-Ashʿarī said existence is identical to the existent. There are two interpretations: some take it literally, meaning that 'existence' is not a real attribute but merely a word, so counting it as an attribute is a concession. The verified scholars, like al-Saʿd al-Taftāzānī, interpret it differently: they say al-Ashʿarī meant that existence is not an extra attribute in external reality that could be seen (i.e., it is not a distinct entity), but it is a conceptual (iʿtibārī) consideration. This is the correct view, and thus counting existence as an attribute is not a concession.

Key Terms
عين الموجود
أمر اعتباري
زائد على الذات
تسامح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think al-Ashʿarī meant literal identity, so existence is exactly the essence in every respect.
Correction: The verified interpretation is that al-Ashʿarī meant existence is not an extra external attribute but a conceptual matter, not literal identity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct understanding of al-Ashʿarī's statement 'al-wujūd ʿayn al-mawjūd'?
A) Existence is a real attribute added to the essence
B) Existence is identical to the existent in external reality
C) Existence is a conceptual matter and not an extra attribute in the external
D) Existence is the same as the essence in all respects

Key Takeaway
The correct Ash'ari position is that existence is a conceptual attribute, not an extra reality added to the essence, so counting it as an attribute involves no concession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this understanding affect the way you think about Tawḥīd and the oneness of Allah? Will you pause before uttering such phrases to reflect on their theological depth?
?
The scholars argued over whether al-Ashʿarī’s statement should be taken literally or interpreted. Are there areas of your faith where you have settled for a superficial understanding, not seeking the deeper, more accurate interpretation? What will you do to rectify that today?
?
If existence is a conceptual attribute, then your own existence is also a conceptual consideration from Allah’s creative will. Does this humble you, realizing that your being is not a separate reality but a contingent conceptual manifestation of Allah’s power? How should this change your daily attitude of arrogance or self-sufficiency?
